THE LAW’S "ANXIETY.”
"The authorities are very anxions about the Mahommedan, Fatta Chand, who, as he believes that dying ignominously by the hands of unbelievers will exclude him from Paradise, refuses
to take food, and if not artificially fed must die before the date fixed for his execution. —Daily Paper.
DEATH BY STRANGULATION (loq.): “Look here, my friend, we can’t have you interfering in matters of this sort.”
DEA TH BY STARVATION (loq.) : “My honourable and learned friend, what’s the odds? he gets there just the same.”
DE ATH BY STRANGULATION : “Perhaps so, but it’s so thoroughly unorthodox, and what’s more, it’s un-English, and we are not going to have the moral effects of
our civilised methods of dealing with criminals destroyed by heathenish and superstitious prejudices.”

SATURDAY, APRIL 25, 1891.
The Progress of the Federal Discussion.
L’Abbe Sieves, who had always a fresh con-
stitution' for" a regenerated France in his
portfolio, 1 was accustomed to reply when told
that the particular scheme which he had pre-
sented for consideration at the moment,
“ wouldn’t do,” that he “ would prepare
another,” It is , now pretty evident that
Griffith, the Sieves of the Australasian
Federal Convention, will be informed by the
Australian thatGhe Constitution Bill—elabor-
ated by him, in Queensland, with so much
care and so much smelling of the lamp, and
which he brought with him in his valise to
Sydney as a necessary preliminary to fashion-
ing it, red-hot, in Convention (much as the
stage-cook compounds under the public eye
the pie built ages before in the property-
man’s mysterious den) will not do at all. It
certainly will not do ; and we do not suspect
that when the negative of the provinces is
communicated to Sir Samuel he will have any
stronger objection to “preparing another”
than his prototype. He will be wise if he
manifest an equal pliancy ; for he cannot be
ambitious of making the “D.S.G.” of North
Queenslanders the universal toast of the
Australian. There are some tributes to his
personal eminence and to his political power
which even a vain politician like Griffith
—and there is no vanity like that of your
cold-blooded man—would not care to see
circulating over a larger area. And in any
case, “ Damn Sam Griffith !” will not ring
so pleasantly from the lips of the coming
generations as “ Another Washington— the
Father of his Country !”
## # ,
It was upon Griffith that the greater
share of the work of preparing the Bill to
create the Commonwealth of Australia fell ;
it is he—and not Parkes —whom posterity
will regard as the master-builder of the
ship ; it was he who guided it from the
stays and off the stocks into deep water ; and
it is he who will have to finesse the craft
through the shallows. In so far as the
creation of the Commonwealth will be the
act of any one man, it will be Griffith s,
for his was, distinctively, the Iggal and con-
structive mind of the Convention. He will
justly claim the honours and rewards of
success if the ship of the new nation,
freighted with richer hopes than were ever
borne by the fabled • argosy, reaches- the fair
haven ; but woe to his name and to his fame
if she prove but a coffin-ship after all!
«- * *
It is to Griffith, then, that the people of
Australia will look for that elucidation of the
Constitution which is to commend it to the ,
Australian mind and aspirations. Parkes
has spoken to his “colony,” Munro to his,
and the greatest admirer of the work of the
Convention must experience, despair at the
futility of both attempts to unravel the knots
of the problem. From Playford, of South
Australia, much of educative value might be
expected but for the consideration that he
will be driven, almost certainly, to an ex-
tremist's defence of the Bill owing to the
critical attitude that Downer, Cockburn
and Kingston .will take towards it ; from
h vsh, of Tasmania, nothing will be received
of an importance beyond that of his own
yardstick. It is Griffith whom Australia
wishes to hear and from whom it has a right
to demand an explanation. As the principal
framer of the Constitution, he must be pre-
pared to defend it, to intimate where it may
be improved, and to indicate what he pro-
poses to do to effect improvements. And
this last, we submit, is the question for him.
He cannot, unless he is giving the lie to his
past, be satisfied with the measure as it is.
He has struggled within his State for the
restriction of Imperialism to innocuous
-dimensions,- and-there-he, fought, as- no ether-
Australian politician, except Berry, fought
within h i. s province, the plutocratic and squat-
tucratic element. Can he, after reflection
upon the Constitution as a whole, assert that
the vigorous instincts which achieved for him
his Queensland renown have been granted
full play in the Convention ?
* * #
Griffith has lost one opportunity of
shaping the Australian sentiment. Let him
not lose another. When he speaks, let him
answer criticism, and not evade it, and, if he
cannot answer, and will not commit an
evasion, let him accept the position. He
permitted himself to yield to temporising
tendencies, the result of which is, that the
Constitution gives a fuller recognition to
Imperialism than has been accorded by
“ Colonial ” practice of the last decade, and
thus he was forced into a false position. Let
him have the courage to retrieve it ; let him
show that, strong man as he was in Conven-
tion, he can still be stronger, and can,
GuLLiVER-like, throw aside the Lilliputian
restraints with which he allowed himself to
be bound. If he will do this, he may save
himself the ignominy of being told to “ pre-
pare another.”
* * it
The one particular on which the Federal
States must be informed before the Federal
compact is entered into—and which, satis-
factorily settled, other difficulties, even those
of the Imperial connection, of tariffs and of
territories, will be adjusted—is State-right.
Who, in the conflict which will as surely
come as night follows the day, will be the
ultimate and final authority ? Jefferson
said the American States “were co-ordinate
departments of a simple and undivided
whole.” Will this be the status of the
Federated Australias, or will they be sub-
ordinate always and in all things to the
Federal Government ? Is the Federal Go-
vernment to be the agent of the Sovereign
States, or their master ? Is the Federal
Government the product of a compact
between the individual provinces, or is it a
party to such compact ? Upon the answer
to these questions will depend the reply to
one other—Can any State nullify an act of
the Federal Parliament, or render void the
administrative procedure 'of the Federal
Government ? The Constitution, it will be
said, already answers this final question, by
ordering “that when a law of the State
is inconsistent with the law of the
Common wealth, the latter is to prevail.”
True, 'it answers it —on paper. Turn
to the discussions in convention, and it
is obvious that this clause was voted en masse
without the slightest significance of its fatal
tendency. The principle of nullification is
the Conservative principle of the Federal
Union, for it implies the right to separate
when injustice is inflicted. If it be de-
signedly abandoned by the several States,
upon a full acquaintance with what is in-
volved in the act of surrender, well and
good; but let us not hastily and thoughtlessly
force on Union when the bloody finger of
history warns us that within that clause
there are the seeds of rebellion and of
internecine strife. A Federal Union
must be, in its essence, indissoluble;
but if it is not to be welded again
by force, after being cleft by the sword,
that principle of nullification must be intelli-
gently and voluntarily surrendered. For
the States to accept now the Constitution
with the second clause of Chapter 5, con-
tained therein, a clause which was voted
blindly and, we dare say, ignorantly, is to
invite civil war. Let Griffith explain if he
can, why this pitfall has been placed on
the Federal path ; if he cannot do it, no other
Conventionist can. And until an explana-
tion be forthcoming we say, “ Hold off! ”
* * *
Parkes, Premier of New South Wales,
has spoken to his constituents, and Munro,
Premier of Victoria, has spoken to his.
Coming fresh from Convention with their
mutual-admiration speeches still ringing in
their ears, it was only to be expected that
two such politicians had nothing but good to
say of their recent work. Unstinted lauda-
tion, of the . Constitution was the charac-
teristic of both deliveries, and neither
speaker considered he was under any obliga-
tion to excuse the proceedings of a body
which had done all in its power to cripple
the Australias to a degree that was beyond
the power of the Imperial Parliament to
do of its own motion. The popular
acceptance of the Constitution must
be an effect of an awakened public
intelligence and an enlarged apprehension by-
Australians of the Federal principle—of its
historic basis, of all that the idea implies, of
all the responsibility it confers, of all the in-
spiring dignity which lies at its core. It
was “ the manifest duty of each Premier”—
the phrase is not ours', but Monro's in Con-
vention—to educate the people he repre-
sented as he had himself been educated—to
put before the ■people of his State his reasons
for his individual adhesion to the Constitu-
tion, his explanation of his conduct in de-
parting from his previously-declared con-
victions, and his new notion of the federal
bond. Tested by this definition, which was
applauded by every Premier of Australasia
as it was uttered, what was Parkes’ speech
at St. Leonards, what was Munro’s at Gee-
long ? They were the outpourings of men
either ignorant or insincere, or both. Parses
declared that the Senate of the Common-
wealth has had for its exemplar “that most
august of legislative bodies, the American
Senate.”
* . .... . , #
Now, the Senate of the American
States perfectly represents the indivi-
duality of the .several States in the
Union, and it derives its august nature
from this fact. But its perfection of
I’epresentative function is the corollary of
its possession of co-ordination of legislative
power with the popular Chamber, or in its
exclusive capacity to revise certain acts of
the Executive, which peculiar attribute
balances the deprivation to which it is sub-
jected with respect to one or two depart-
ments of legislation. The Senate of the
Commonwealth will occupy no such repre-
sentative position, for co-ordination of power
and function is denied it by the Constitu-
tion, and a piece of gear constructed instead
which, in our judgment, will, by reason of
its inherent imperfection, throw the whole
machinery of the Government into disorder.
For the New South Wales Premier to assert
that the two Senates are endowed with
analogous capacities and occupy similar posi-
tions, is utterly at variance with the certain-
ties of the case. Than this statement nought
could be more mischievous, or, if made in-
sincerely, more immoral. We acquit him,
however, in this instance of an attempt t j
wilfully mislead the people, for the function
of the States, as expressed by the Senate in
U.S. Federalism, is, perhaps, the most deli-
cate'known to any existing legislative system,
and Parkes’ order of mhid is such that he
is never.to be depended upon for a true
perception of what is exactly delimited or
finely discriminated. As a putter-forth of
vague, resonant and pretentious generalisa-
tions, these provinces do not own his equal ;
he has an unrivalled faculty for seeing the
superficial and cruder aspects of a question ;
but in the quality of intellect which is
essential to the detection of differences in
things which seem like, he is absolutely
deficient. No man, however, can be a safe
guide in the intricacies of legislation, and
especially in the peculiarly-delicate business
of Constitution-making, who keeps on the
surface. It is in the depths that the sunken
rocks lie, and the American Union was nearly
wrecked because the Northern States adhered
to the implied significance of a phrase in its
Constitution, and the Southern States the
plain, superficial meaning. Parkes is in-
capable of educating the people on the true
nature and ramifications of federal institu-
tions, because he himself has yet to undergo
a process of further education. He lias
learned much since he made his Tentertield
speech in 1889 ; for he has learned that the
particular Federal Constitution he then out-
lined has no chance of being called into
existence. Whatever other faults the
Commonwealth Constitution has. it is not a
replica of the Canadian. Parkes, knowing
this, may yet learn more.
* # #
As with Parkes, so with Munro. His
conception of Federation would be puerile if
it were not for his office. Since, however,
he is not merely Munro, but Premier of
Victoria, his words must be discussed as
seriously as the circumstances will permit.
In his speech at Geelong, he was apparently
resolved to leave Parkes the honour of being
pre-eminently silly. Yet Parkes’ notion of
senatorial functions was wisdom itself com-
pared with Munko’s idea of how the safety-
valve of the Constitution—the power of
amendment—is to be applied, or with his
sense of the status of the Federal Capital.
Dismissing the latter point in a couple of
sentences, we will ask, What is to be the
function of the Capital ? Is it not to be the
seat of the Central Government ? Then, it
is patent that it must not be overawed by
local aggregation of wealth or population, if
the Government is to do justice and preserve
equity as between the States. Freeman,
the one Englishman whose contributions to
the literature of the Federal principle are
monumental, declares that in nothing was
the wisdom of the framers of the American
Constitution made so conspicuous or more jus-
tified by the issue than in making the seat of
authority a ] urely Federal district. The
direct and indirect independence of the
peculiar federal territory is imperative to
the safe working of the Constitution, and
the Convention were as distinctly oblivious
to what was demanded from them in refer-
ing the selection of a site for the capital to
the Federal Parliament as they were in
refusing to make the “ One man one vote ”
the basic principle of the Federation, It is
not a subordinate issue, hut a main one ;
and Munro would settle it by making Gse-
long—a suburb of Melbourne—the seat of
Commonwealth authority ! Geelong also has
the disadvantage that it would be accessible
to a British force when one is sent to
emulate the noble example set in the burn-
ing of Washington !
* # #
But Munro excels himself and beats even
Parkes hollow when he conies to elucidate
the capacity of the States to amend the
Constitution. It is the Victorian Premier’s
wish, and that of the majority of his
colleagues in the Convention that the States
shall accept the Bill as it now stands,
and that any amendments deemed desirable
by any individual , State shall be sub-
mitted to the Federal Parliament thence to
be decided upon ! When the Constitution
is adopted by the people, the chapter in the
Bill thus providing for amendment may be
regarded as historically and theoretically
sound, but to propose to bring the same
routine into operation before the Bill becomes
law is surely a suggestion that ought to be
laughed out of the discussion, and could only
have occurred to a Conventionist who had
never taken the trouble to understand the
rudimentary operation of the principle sought
to-be expressed in the Bill. The State Con-
ventions which are to be called to adopt the
Constitution—and to consider it ! —may,
Munro acquiesces, suggest amendments,
but they can only accept the Bill as
it now stands, and then submit their
amendments to the Federal Parliament.
This plan could only have any value if the
Parliament had power to forthwith incor-
porate the amendments with the Constitu-
tion. But such power is designedly with-
held from it—and properly. All that the
Parliament could do, the majority of both
Houses coinciding, and the Governor-General
being I willing , would be to send the amend-
ments for adoption —to whom ? To
the very State Conventions from whom
the amendments had emanated ! The
genius of circumlocution never conceived a
better method of how not to do it.
For the' State Conventions to amend,
and to place their amendments, if
any, before the State Parliament, thence
to be sent to a second or a third Federal
Convention, by which plan the Federal Par-
liament would not be constituted till there
was, at least, an approximation to a perfect
Constitution, would be too simple a plan for
the Victorian Premier or—there is an
alternative—too honest. To follow the
Munro idea would be to indefinitely post-
pone the amendment of the Constitution in
the radical, the democratic, the Australian
direction. That is, perhaps, its object, for
it is approved by the Argus, which never
yet placed its seal of approval on any pro-
posal that designed to give effect to the
popular will. The Argus imprimatur damns
it as antagonistic to democracy.
Freetrade or Federation—Which?
The Pakkes Government has obviously got its
war-whoop ready for the approaching New
South Wales general election, and its cry will
be Freetrade and Australian Federation. There
is no visible connection between Freetrade and
Federation—in fact, under present conditions
the two principles, or rather the one principle
and a fetich, are distinctly antagonistic—but
for several years past it has been apparent that
Freetrade cannot stand alone, and it is necessary
to find something for that pne-legged creed to
lean up against. It has leaned upon a good
many things at various times, but it is a notable
circumstance that it never leaned upon the
same article twice. A few years ago it bor-
rowed the Almighty to shore up Parkes, and
the statement went forth that the Protestant
faith would come to grief if a tax were put on
M‘Millan’s calico ; and it was understood that
the Lord would be indignant if the imported
hardware business was discouraged, and would
give over New South Wales to the dominion
of the Pope. When this dank and flabby
lie died a natural death, the Mongol was
pressed into the service of the Good Cause, and
the apostles of Free Competition travelled for a
while on the unsupported assertion that they
alone, among all the men on earth, w'ere fitted
to prevent the free competition of the cheap
Chinaman, and that Freetrade in general meant
Protection against the objectionable Asiatic.
The statement was obviously a glaring false-
hood, for the imposition of a prohibitive cus-
toms duty on the Mongolian was an outrage on
every axiom of the Foreign-trade gospel, but
Parkes’s hair carried it through successfully.
Even the Chinaman, however, is worn out now,
and as a bald and naked policy of Freetrade
would be howled down with derision in every
electorate throughout New South Wales, the
party will travel in the present instance on the
tale of Australian Federation. And when it
gets started it will assert, with a glad and
joyous disregard of facts and dates and common
intelligence, that the country must take Parkes
and Freetrade and Federation all together,
because if it doesn’t have Freetrade it can’t
have Federation, and if it doesn’t take Parkes
it can’t have either of them, and if it doesn’t
have the whole three it will go to
smash. At the present moment it is
difficult to decide whether New South Wales
will notice the discrepancy or not. The
assertion is as conspicuously absurd as any
merely human statement can be; and if the
party declared that a man without feet couldn t
have a hat, and that if he hadn’t a boil on his
back he couldn’t have either feet or a hat, it
would keep quite as close to the region of


accuracy ; but, nevertheless, the New South
Wales Freetrade party will make the assertion,
and the result remains to be seen.
# * #
Therefore New South Wales will be dis-
figured from east to west and from north to
south with bills calling on the nation to vote for
Parkes and Freetrade and Federation, and the
Freetrade statistician, with his wide, untravelled
wastes of assurance and his endless store of
mendacity, will point to the Protectionist as a
soulless wretch who is blocking the way of
Australian unity. By the constant iteration of
this conspicuous lie a few weak-kneed Protec-
tionists will be induced to vote against Federa-
tion merely in order to differentiate themselves
from their opponents, and thus a certain air of
probability will bo lent to an assertion which is
badly in want of some such assistance. There
is only one Foreign-trade party, and Parkes is
its prophet, and consequently that astute old
man will get the votes of the whole scanty rem-
nant of the Freetraders, whether they believe
in Federation or not. He will also prospect for
the votes of any Protectionists who value
Federation more than Protection, and who are
too obtuse to recognise that Federation is pos-
sible with Protection, and absolutely impossible
with Freetrade. Ho will further look after the
suffrages of the labouring classes by pointing
out that the Protectionists only support the
principle of “One Man One Vote,” while he is
prepared to go the whole political hog and
advocate the policy of “ One Woman One Vote ”
in addition ; and many unthinking and illiterate
electors will fail to see that this addition is
merely a guarantee that the Upper House
will reject the whole scheme, whereby the
multiple vote of property will be saved
and yet the hypocritical Parkes will gain
the credit of having done his best to lose
it. And in addition to all this the Govern-
ment party will do its best to split up the
hostile vote by putting up bogus opponents
so as to divide the enemy’s camp into Federal
Protectionists and anti-Federal Protectionists,
Federal and anti-Federal Labour candidates,
and the like ; for though Parkes favours the
system of election by majorities only, he has
kept that theory on hand to whoop about at
the coming election, instead of passing it into
law for his own undoing. Among all these
chances the Parkes Cabinet has got a better
show for the future than appeared possible a
few weeks ago, and if the Federal excitement
can be kept up—provided there is any such
excitement at all —Sir Henry may be able by-
and-bye to throw ail objectionable colleagues
overboard and start afresh.
# # #
Should Parkes return with a majority, how-
ever, trouble will begin shortly afterwards, and
it will be necessary to cast a good many prin-
ciples into the sea to lighten the ship. The
grand theory of one vote, and one vote only, for
each inhabitant will probably be passed with
acclamation by the Assembly, and be allowed
to go to wreck in the Council for want of a
little timely pressure ; and then the nation can-
not reasonably jump on the Assembly, seeing
that body did its level best, and it cannot jump
on the Council, for that dejected museum is
inaccessible to popular indignation. The pro-
ject of election by absolute majorities only will
either go the same road, or else be smothered
and talked out while Parkes is absent owing to
his leg, which will probably develop new symp-
toms about that time ; and when he is brought'
down on a shutter to make a noble effort in the
interests of the democratic cause it will be too
late, And as to the two great axioms of Free-
trade and Federation, one of them must go, for
the two cannot exist together ; and which will
be sacrificed depends entirely on circumstances,
If the Australian Commonwealth is established,
then the represen tatiAms of Victoria, Queens-
land, South Australia, and Tasmania will
constitute a solid majority for Protection, and
the greating importing interest is a lost and
ruined and shoddy business for evermore. The
question is Commonwealth versus Calico
Jimniyism, and the result is doubtful and un-
certain.
# # #
There are various ivays of effecting a com-
promise under such Circumstances. One simple
and effectual method is by passing the Federa-
tion Bill Avith the addition of some utterly im-
possible stipulation in favour of Freetrade, and
when the other provinces refuse to accept this
proviso, the importers can curse at Protection
tor ever afterwards, on the ground that it, and
it alone, wrecked the glorious Commonwealth.
This scheme was adopted with considerable
success some years ago, and very few appeared
to realise that the result would have been just
the same if Ncav South Wales had demanded
the establishment of polygamy as the one condi-
tion on which it would join the Federal Council,
and had thereafter denounced the sinful mono-
gamous policy which hail ruined the nation. In
this case Ncav South , Wales Avill lose Federa-
tion, and save Freetrade and Parkes. On the
other hand, however, Parkes may think it best
to put the Federation Bill in front, and carry
it with the assistance of any available Protec-
tionists and such members of his own surprised
and disgusted party as will Mloav him, and
then take the chance of being the first Prime
Minister of all Australia. In such a situation
his own following of importers, lawyers, and
landlords Avould nave to submit through sheer
necessity, for to eject Parkes would be merely
to bring in Protection with a rush, while to
allow him to remain in office Avould at least
postpone the evil day until the Federal tariff
oanie into operation. In that case New South
Wales will lose Freetrade, but it will, at least,
save Federation and Parkes— in fact, if the
next election can be tided over, the State is
bound to save Parkes under all circumstances.
a third device is to endeavour to pass the
Federation Bill and whoop for Freetrade at the
same time—in fact, to pass it as a great Free-
h’ade measure, and trust that the dire stupidity
°f the shoddy party might prevent them recog
nising the obvious fact that Federation was, in
their case, only another name for destruction.
be party which could be sold so outrageously
18 capable °f any foolishness under the sun, but
still the scheme might succeed, and when the
inevitable disaster came, Parkes would pro-
bably have started a new career as a Protec-
tionist senator of the Commonwealth, and
would bo right out of reach.
# * #
Owing to various reasons, however, these
alternatives will not be laid before the electors
at present. For if the situation were explained in
all its bald and naked infamy the nation must
be divided into two sharply-defined sections —
a Freetrade party, sworn to oppose Federation
to the bitter end in the interests of foreign
imports; and a Protectionist party, which
might or might not support Federation, accord-
ing to the views of the majority of its members.
The first party would not want Parkes, because
he is a Federationist, and the second would
have no use for him because he is a Freetrader
—in fact, there would be no possible or con-
ceivable demand for Parkes anywhere. Then
either the Freetrade party must win under a
new leader, or else the Protectionist party
must come to the front, and instead of
Parkes, its qeader, if it had a leader, would
be in a position to bid for the first posi-
tion in the political world of Australia. With
this overwhelming difficulty in view Sir Henry
judiciously refrains from needless explanations.
If he can run Freetrade and Federation in
double-harness he will have at least a faint
prospect of success ; and if he could also secure
the vigorous and undivided support of the pub-
licans and the teetotallers, the priests, the
Orange lodges, Abigail, and the Cardinal, his
chances would be considerable. Unfortunately,
however, there is not time to hunt up all
these discordant elements and offer the whole
earth to each of them, and therefore Parkes will
go before the country at the next opportunity as a
disunited apostle of unity and a Freetrader who
desires to pass as the originator of a Protec-
tionist Commonwealth and take his chance in
these characters. If fortune attends him it will
be the last time the old man will want his obse-
quious party, and he doesn’t care what happens
to them afterwards. If the country will endorse
the Federation policy at the polls, and the
Federation Bill can be got through the Legisla-
ture by dint of hard coalescing with anybody
or everybody, or by any other means whatso-
ever, then Parkes will go away and be a
Federal statesman and have a long and dignified
rest from his old associates ; and if it doesn’t go
through it may at least help to prop up the
Freetrade party when it would otherwise have
gone under. For though Parkes may kick his
own party now under the impression that he is
done with it, yet if there be no Federation after
all he will come back and use it just as before,
and the kicking will be dropped into oblivion.
And if the whole Parkes faction collapses at
the polls like a snowball struck by a burning
comet, he will at least die for Federation instead
of Freetrade, which is an obvious advantage
seeing that he may resurrect again on the
Federal ticket, while Gehazi himself would get
up out of his tomb before the Freetrade ticket
could rise from its oblivion without something
stronger than itself to help it up.
Cool Cheek.
We desire to focus public attention for a few
moments on the school of law that has lately
been added as a new attraction to Sydney
University, The Chancellor, Sir William
Manning, is a lawyer—at any rate, he was a
judge—and naturally he has taken the legal
nursery under his especial charge. But he
isn’t satisfied with it. In his annual Commemo-
ration address he complained that it would not
fulfil half of its intended mission for the
advancement of legal learning and for the
elevation of the legal professions unless
rules were made by the judges for the
compulsory training of articled clerks under
appropriate portions of its curriculum. It
appears that the University has communi-
cated with the judges, who have not yet found
time to meet on the subject; but Sir William
hopes 1 ‘ that effective regulations may soon be
made, so that the benefits of the systematic
legal education afforded by the University may
be made to extend to this branch of the pro-
fession.” To put this open-handed benevolence
beyond the reach of suspicions Sir William
went further into particulars :
1 will also frankly state, in the interests of the Uni-
versity, that our school of law will be but poorly attended,
and will be in danger of languishing if confined to candi-
dates for the bar ; and such few candidates for the other
branch as may go through the whole course without
special encouragement, of whom, as I hear, there are
already some. I must also avow that the University
needs,'as in Victoria, the fees of the articled clerks to
contribute to the cost of the department, and to the
general administration of the University.
“Special encouragement,” which we have
taken the liberty of underlining, is distinctly
good. It is his Honor’s humorous way of refer-
ring to the compulsory rules by which the
articled clerks are to be rounded up into the
University lecture-rooms.
# * #
To put it straight, the drift of the conspiracy
in which the Senate wants the judges to join
is this : The University has imported an expen-
sive professor and started an elaborate system
for the instruction of sucking lawyers, but the
prospective brigands find they can do very well
without any such instruction. A youngster
who wants to become a lawyer, and who is
willing to work hard and read hard, can
learn his business just as effectually in the
office of a firm with a good connection as by
listening to the lectures of gentlemen whose
practice at the bar leaves them leisure to teach
in the law-school. But the University wants
money to pay its professor and lecturing-staff,
so the judges are asked to make rules to compel
articled clerks to pay the fees and attend
lectures whether they want them or not. For
serene effrontery the learned councillors are
hard to beat. The competition in the legal
profession, and the ever-working law of the
survival of the fittest, which wrought so that
James Martin, who began as an office-boy and
was made a present of his articles, died as Chief
Justice, may be trusted to make law students
take eager advantage of everything that will
give them a better chance in the race. If a
course at the University law school will put a
man ahead of those who never saw a University,
the law school will attract pupils, and be a
success. If it won’t do that, there is no reason
why pupils should go there, and to coerce them
there in order to put fees into the pockets of
the professor, or keep the University from drop,:
ing money over its new spec., would be shame-
less robbery.
* * *
What does it matter to the judges where a
lawyer learns his trade, so long as he knows it?
And what has it to do with the judges, any-
how ? The people who employ the lawyers are
the parties interested, and they are much more
concerned about the way their man does his
work than about the particular hall-mark he
bears. The judges have the power at present
of specifying what an articled clerk has to
know, and how much of it he must know, before
they let him commence preying on his own
account as a full-toothed solicitor, and that is
quite sufficient say for them to have in the
matter. If there were no examination at all,
the man who was best up in his work would, in
the long run, get the most work, as he does
now ; and the only way the Judges have of find-
ing out whether a man is up to Ids work or not
is by examination. If the examination be a
satisfactory test of a candidate’s fitness, then
no one will get through who is not fit, Univer-
sity lectures or no lectures ; if it be not a satis-
factory test, it ought to be abolished. In any
case, why should a man who has brains and
energy enough to master his trade without
spending time or money at the University be
compelled to spend these merely to bolster up
a crazy and, so far, “ fashionable ” monopoly ?
* # #
Chancellor Manning says that part of the
intended misson of the law school is “the ele-
vation of the legal profession.” That is one of
these pretty, class phrases that modern demo-
cracy is beginning to find out of late. What
have the public to do with the elevation of the
legal professions ? Why should the public be
taxed to put an extra coat of varnish on the
pretensions of any caste or class ? The eleva-
tion of the bricklaying profession is just as much
a matter of State concern as that of elevating-
lawyers. All we have to do with a bricklayer
is to see he lays his bricks properly, and a
lawyer has fulfilled his functions when
he wins our case or induces an intelligent
jury of our countrymen to find us not
guilty. He will not have any delicacy about
making us pay for that, and we have no
particular call to contribute to the expense of
his “elevation.” Furthermore, we know of
old what the elevation of a profession means in
the mouths of Mr. Chancellor Manning and
his class. It means the shutting out as far as
possible of everyone who hasn’t either a grand-
father or a full purse. It means the monopo-
lising of lucrative occupations by high-toned
and well-connected people whose names are
known at Darling Point and Toorak. It means
the repression of upstart brains, whose owners,
like Judge Real, in Queensland, lave an
ambition to work their way from the carpenter’s
bench to the bench of the Supreme Court.
# # #
There may lie many a young man now quali-
fying for a profession, and with a good chance
of getting into the front rank, who would be
snuffed out by a regulation compelling him to
attend University lectures in Sydney and pay
lecture fees. If he can study in [the country,
and present himself for examination when he is
ready, that will be the time for his University-
bred competitor to show his superiority. No
doubt it would be a good thing for the present
members of the lawyers’ guild to put new
difficulties in the way of the crowds who are
flocking in every term ; but the public interests
are best served by wholesome competition ; and
if anyone is to be snuffed out, let it be the
noodles with money and no brains, rather than
the men whose brains form their only stock-in-
trade.
* # #
The University senates of course adopt the
opposite principle, and will go on adopting it till
an earthquake of some sort rouses them to learn
that the University is a national concern, and
not their private show, The New South Wales
judges, who have the decision of this business,
made a hopeful show of common-sense a few
months ago when they abolished at one swoop
most of the restrictions that had been ac-
cumulating round the entrance-gate to the Bar.
If they display a reactionary spirit now, and do
anything to make the solicitors’ trade-union
more a monopoly of the culchawed classes than
it is at present, they will open the question why
that particular union should receive any more
legal recognition than other bodies of the same
sort. That is a question which it might be
useful for the Trades and Labor Council to look
into just now. The application of the “free-
dom of contract” doctrine to the case of the
man who wants Reconveyance drawn or a libel-
action fought, would put several hundred solici-
tors and barristers into prompt sympathy with
the shearers on strike at Barcaldine.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Man and the Gallows—A Race.
In Melbourne Gaol a race is in progress between
a man and a gallows, and the authorities are
much vexed and harassed by the fact that the
gallows is dropping behind. Consequently they
are considering the advisability of giving a lift
to the ancient implement of strangulation, or
fooling with the weights, or falsifying the han-
dicap, for all the public money has been piled
on that blood-stained object, and the man and
the gallows are both nearing their final turning-
point and only the last furlong of the race
remains to be run. Fatta Chand, Hindoo,
convicted of homicide on circumstantial evi-
dence, has a deep-rooted objection to Australian
modes of execution, having been taught by his
creed that he will be a lost and restless spirit
through all eternity if he dies ignominiqusly by
the hands of unbelievers, and therefore he is
trying to perish by self-starvation as a matter
of preference. The law and the hangman are
naturally afraid under these circumstances that
he may die ere he can be officially butchered,
and some indecent individual connected with
the administration of “justice” has even sug-
gested that he should be hanged a week earlier
than liis time, iest the gallows should lose the
race. Nobody kicked this person apparently,
or raised any strong objection to his brutality—
iufact, the Executive Council seriously considered
the proposal, and came within an ace of accept-
ing it. Finally, however, it was resolved that it
was undignified for the gallows to rush and
gallop and perspire in order to overtake any-
body, and so Fatta Chand is being violently
fed and kept alive by a conference of doctors
that there may be enough of him left to strangle
when the occasion arrives. According to
modern ideas Fatta Chand is to be executed as
a precautionary measure, on the ground that he
will never kill anybody else after he is dead,
and from this point of view it seems to be
nobody’s business how he dies. Also he is to
serve as a warning to others, and his death by
voluntary starvation should be a sufficiently
awful event to warn anybody or anything; in
fact, if it will not fill the bill then capital
punishment is a dead and wretched failure.
And in the third place he must be killed because
the law wants to get level with him for its own
satisfaction, and if his death by hunger won’t
satisfy the law, then that same law is only fit for
a race of ghouls and cannibals, and is totally unfit
for an age of so-called civilisation. Probably,
however, the Government and the Bench and
the hangman are all too thick-headed to reason
these matters out. They have only one idea in
the place which they mistake for their intelli-
gence, and that is that some respectable old
precedent will be burst up if Fatta Chand does
not perish in the orthodox manner ; and Fatta
Chand objects to perishing in the orthodox
manner because he believes that should he do
so, some kind of Asiatic hell will open and take
him in to all eternity. The other parties to the
transaction meanwhile do not care much whether
the wretched Hindoo believes he is going to his
own particular hell or not; they merely want to
hang him and let him go where he may. There-
fore, the race between the man and the gallows
is an exciting one, for the man is running for
his one and only chance of eternal salva-
tion, and the gallows is running for the
preservation of its old, blood-stained tradi-
tions, and its ancient inhumanity, and for
the glorious memories of the hangman and the
soaped noose, and the drop, and the dull, hollow
thud, and the six columns of gory drivel in the
daily papers. If the Governor and the Govern-
ment know even the first rudiments of common
humanity, they can do a. kindly and generous
action by allowing this unhappy Asiatic to
starve in peace ; but then, unhappily, the daily
press makes the Government, and an influential
section of the press has risen up unanimously to
demand its gallows and its “ thud,” and if the
thud isn’t forthcoming there will be trouble at
an early date.
“ The Bulletin ” Refers Back.
Melbourne Age, in a recent leader, had not
only the impudence to brag of its ridiculous
“ Russian scare ” which “ led to the reconstruc-
tion of our defence system,” but the sinful
audacity to declare that it was the first paper
to direct public attention to the Chinese ques-
tion, and so secure Australia to the Australians.
This last assertion will disgust even the “ con-
stant reader ” of the Age, who must know—if
he knows anything—that The Bulletin with
Phil May’s cartoon, an article appearing in
the same issue, and, subsequently, its Anti-
Chinese Number was responsible for the arrest
of the Chow invasion. The Bulletin put its
foot down upon the leper at a time when his
alleged virtues of “industry and thrift” were
catchwords with the Melbourne press. A proof
that The Bulletin then stood alone in fighting
against the yellow disease may be found in a
far back number of the Argus. Not many
months before that stupid old Tory organ veered
round to the popular side it went out of its
lumbering way to sneer at the Anti-Chinese
gospel as “the stock-in-trade of a disreputable
Sydney newspaper. ” Aline which The Bul-
letin is proud to reprint firstly because the
venomous enmity of the Argus carries a com-
pliment with it, and secondly, as a reminder
that the now settled policy of this continent
was originally identified with The Bulletin’s
red cover, and maliciously described as the only
visible means of support of the most widely-
circulated publication in Australia. This para-
graph administers one in the ribs to Argus
stupidity, and a couple of real stingers to the
Age’s lying jaw.
Another Lesson from America.
It should make apathetic Australians blush to
read that there ■was bloodshed at the mayoral
election, in Chicago. Here, as in England,
municipal appointments are left to the manage-
ment of small cliques of shopkeepers, who take
a petty interest in electing the most wind-baggy
councillors they can procure whilst the vast
majority of the ratepayers are content to stand
aside and sneer. The average citizen seems to
find an absurd pleasure in reading of how his
local “ representatives ” have been flourishing
their fat fists at one another, and when they
rest awhile from the usual indulgence in bad
language, he (the average idiot) shakes his sides
over the humorous supposition that “ they’ve
got another blank swindle amongst themselves.”
He delights to amuse his domestic circle with
little things he has just heard concerning the
new Town Hall job, which will saddle posterity
with debt for the next century or so. It
affords him infinite satisfaction to mention
that Fizgig —a man he wouldn’t be seen in a
five-acre paddock with—will soon be sitting on
the Bench, no doubt. In short, the average
citizen makes it his business to assist, by not
voting at all himself, the election of councillors
at whom he can cast contempt. Nothing suits
him better than to call one's attention to the
beastly roads, filthy drains, and bad street-
lighting in his district, and then ask “What
else can be expected from such a crowd as ive’vc
got ?” This lack of interest in local government
was brought out from “home” by our fore-
fathers, and has grown as fast as the geraniums.
Ancient corporations of Hin gland are but very
little more respectable than their offspring, the
Australian councils. The awful reverence with
which distinguished foreigners speak of My Lor’
Mayor of London is a standing joke among the
very Cockneys who could, if they chose, make
his position as honourable in reality as it looks
. on paper.


A Friend of Labour.
Sib Samuel Griffith, a politician who is sup-
posed, by the Conservative press, to be hanging
somewhere on the dizzy brink of Socialism,
Communism, and Anarchy, has just issued
another of these solemn pronouncements by
which he keeps up the hopes of Labour with-
out doing any serious damage to Capital.
ihe statement in question is part of a Bill
or introduction in the Queensland Legis-
lature-one of those “ declaratory measures”
m winch Sir Samuel is accustomed to assert a
principle without any corresponding practice,
and to announce something ought to be done
without doing it. It is a mere abstract moral
observation which is to be solemnly carried
t iiough the usual Parliamentary stages, in the
same manner as the Sermon on the Mount might
be brought in and read a first, second, and
a third time, and duly sanctioned by the Gover-
nor, and embodied in the Statute Book, and
ihen left there to lie forgotten ; and it will do
no good to anybody— except, perhaps, Sir
Samuel Griffith himself. The following is
ilio pompous declaration in question :
OS is'o r, f i. U ‘"‘, an , d Vr T i : nieasln cof ' va ?es is such a sum
uhirh A immediate recompense for the labour for
'hm f PaR ’ regard to its character and
such ris’ii - Ca, l " ever . be Ulk ' n at a less sum than
sue,!! a.-, in sufficient to maintain the labourer and his
family in a state of health and reasonable comfort
Xo more infamous bosh than this was ever
passed on a nation of illiterate cannibals. For
every employer, even though be pays only one
shilling for eighteen hours’ toil, maintains, and
always has maintained, that the shilling is “a
fair and immediate recompense for the labour
for w Inch it is paid,” and as there is nothing in
‘ Sir Griffith’s revolutionary assertion
to contradict him things are left absolutely as
they were. And even if the wages paid will
only supply the labourer and his family
with a leaky pig-sty, and a bed of mouldy
straw, and a dry crust, the employer re-
plies that the pig-sty and the straw and
die crust are reasonable comfort for a labourer ;
and the noble theories of Samuel of Wales say
nothing definite to the contrary. For that
mighty driveller says nothing—absolutely
nothing—that is not already admitted by every-
body ; and he might as reasonably profess to
alleviate human suffering by enacting that no
man s land should be on top of the land of
another man, lest the man underneath should
be damaged while endeavouring to live on his
property, as attempt to reform the earth by
heavy platitudes which come out at the same
hole they went in at. And, moreover, even if
Griffiths unhinged and incapable assertion
meant what it pretends to mean, it is only
commonplace Conservatism at the best. There
18 “natural and proper measure of wages,”
as Mr pAMUEL employs the term, in existence.
There w no “ measure ” of any kind, in fact,
for the only reform which can ever do the world
any permanent good is the abolition of wages,
and the establishment of a co-operative system
under which the community will be every man’s
employer and paymaster. So long as wages last,
they are an unfixable quantity ; their measure
is just as much as the one party is able to get,
and just as little as the other party can manage
to give. Lnder present conditions Premier
Griffith might as well undertake to arbitrate
between the lion and the traveller, and decide
iow much of the latter ought reasonably to be
eaten and how much should be allowed to go
on to its destination. The one question is
quite as capable of being settled by his officious
interference as the other.
Some “ Great Powers.”
Ihe mean and foolish squabble between Italy
and the United States which fills so much space
m the daily papers is growing wearisome. It is
a dismal story of how two of the Great Powers
of the earth howl impotently at each other, and
yet as neither can possibly reach the other they
can only howl and nothing more. The warlike
Australian “loyalist ” whose constant cry of
wailing and lamentation goes up to heaven con-
cerning the horrors of Australian independence
and the annexation of the future Australian
republic by the military nations of Europe, has
here a favourable opportunity to size up the
actual power and value of the typical military
state ; and the party which demands that Aus-
tralia should make a martial exhibition of itself
on the Himalayas, or some other frozen moun-
tain range in Asia, might do well to reconsider
that pompous policy, and recover its second
wind. If Italy, with its army of 860,000 men,
and its ponderous ironclads and its huge re-
serves, should declare war against the United
Mates, it cannot start its troops across the
Atlantic, because its ships can’t carry enough
men to take a tenth-rate American city. It
can t send its fleet there either, because the
(H-eat Power is so hopelessly dead-broke that it
can hardly buy coal, unless Humbert puts
his crown jewels up the Roman spout : and
even if it got the fleet by any means on
the American coast, it might bombard some
city, but if it landed a single man he
would be seized the instant he got ashore,
and made to take shares in a' fraudulent
mining company, and ruined in calls. It can’t
plunder American merchant vessels, either, and
thus make the transaction pay, for America has
no vessels that are worth plundering—in fact
it can do nothing but shake its fist by cable-
gram at the republic, and let things rest. And
on the other hand, the United States, despite
its population of 55,000,000 souls, and its
enormous wealth, can’t invade Italy, for it has
no army, and its navy has a large hole in it and
is covered with barnacles. It might buy a new
navy ready made, but it couldn’t buy men to
work it: and the war would be ancient history
before it had a trained naval force ready. It
can’t plunder Italy’s commerce for there is so
little that it couldn’t be found, and to sum the
whole matter up, the entire Italio-American
quarrel is a hollow thing, and is altogether null
and void, because neither party could commit
enough rapine and bloodshed to disturb the
other, however much it tried. The one nation
lias the largest cash surplus and the other
almost the largest army in the world, and both
rank among the greatest powers on earth, and
jet they could hardly he more impotent if each
iiad only one toothless cripple ami a dog. The
military states of Europe are able to put on
considerable airs so long as they only bang each
other, and if an enemy is near enough to be hit
n ith the thick end of the gun they can do
tremendous damage, but at long range they are
not halt so deadly as an asylum for lost infants.
Ihk situation at present between the Queens-
land shearers and the pastoralists is just this,
the two parties arrived at a mutual agreement
alter an infinity of strife and wrangling, and
now the pastoralists insist on substituting for
u another thing which they also call a “mutual
agreement. Only, as the pastoralists drew up
the new agreement entirely on their own respon-
sibility, and without consulting the men, there
ns nothing “ mutual ” about it. The men
demand the old agreement, and the squatters
reply that the new one is practically the same
as the old, therefore, the men must knock under
iind accept it. The men reply that it is entirely
different, and, therefore, that they will not
accept it. Unless the pastoralists are telling
unmitigated lies, the question naturally arises --
i, * y (I|<l tllc y fight tooth and nail against the
old agreement, seeing it was just the same as
the one which they are now fighting for? And
a ong with this comes another problem—lf
there be no real difference between the two sets
ot regulations, why do the pastoralists fight
now . 1 hey can start any day on the old
system, and if the new system be just the same,
then they have nothing to fight about, and all
the police and soldiers and artillery are sheer
idiocy. Ihe position of the men is intelligible ;
they assert that the agreement which the
pastoralists seek to force upon them involves a
serious loss, and they resist the innovation in
consequence ; but as the squatters declare that
the same innovation brings them no profit, then
they are fighting about nothing, and arguing
about nothing, and trying to grab nothing. And
nhen they aren’t fighting about anything, then
they must be fighting out of sheer cussedness,
and Queensland can’t afford to pay £lOOO a day
m war-expenses for the sake of anybody’s
cussedness. J
Tiie failure of the Victorian loan means one
thing only—the syndicate racket is played out
and A is time to go in for “ freedom of con-
tract. If Australia is ass enough to keep on
borrowing money, and making railway lines
whose one purpose is to buy votes and to boom
up lands belonging to politicians and capitalists,
it is a dead certainty that England is ass enough
to lend it, for England lends money to every-
body—in fact, it once lent money to a country
that didn t exist, and the affable individual who
started the idea skipped with the whole sub-
scription. But hitherto the bonds have been
sold to sundry gangs of hook-nosed financiers,
who bought them up cheap, and then unloaded
on the general public at a big profit, and the
idea of simply selling them over the counter at
the Agent-GeneraTs office to anybody who
wanted them, and trading off one at a time if
necessary never seems to have occurred to any-
body. T here is no earthly necessity for paying
commissions and “ accrued interest ” and other
small swindles, neither is there any need to
watch the market and wait for a ‘ ‘ favourable op-
portunity,” which simply means waiting till the
syndicates see a chance to take in the British
speculator—all that is required is that the safe
m the Agent-General’s office should be strong
enough to hold the unsold bonds securely and
time will do the rest. If the market be weak
the bonds will sell slowly—if it be strong they
will sell rapidly, and in either case the syndi-
cates can be done without. If we want to
save Australia a good many millions we should
instruct the Agents-General to kick or other-
wise insult every banker, big financier, or
syndicate that has hitherto lent money to the
provinces, and thus establish an incurable breach
that will prevent them lending a single cent,
for ever afterwards. Then a new system must
be started or else borrowing must come to an
end, and either of these alternatives would be
an undisguised blessing.
Henniker Heaton was one of the crowd of
lory mediocrities who voted in the Commons
tor the continuance of the brutal and infamous
law by which an Englishman who goes on strike
is liable to be arrested and convicted as a
common felon. An magistrate named Bom-
pas has discovered that if any man leaves his
employment because the hours are too long, or
the wages too low, or because he is required to
do two men’s work for less than one man’s pay,
and incites another man to leave along with
him, they can both be sentenced to a long term
ot imprisonment for conspiracy and also—ye
gods !—for intimidation. The British House of
Commons decided by a solid majority that this
law was a fair and impartial one, and shouldn’t
be altered, and Henniker voted with the
majority. A little later on an attempt was
nuide to interfere with the “liberty of the
subject,” and fool with the great principle of
‘freedom of contract” by prohibiting railway
servants being worked for 14, 16, and even
18 hours a day, and when this proposition was
also violently cast out, Henniker was again
found whooping with the Tory majority. God
bless the representative Australian ! Bless him
tor Ins ungrammatical grovel, and his bull-
headed Toryism, and his other joyous qualities !
Bless him tor his ghastly attempts to look like
a line old English gentleman by helping to grind
the faces of the poor, and by treading with his
big, damp, coarse feet on the hungry ! And
above all things, bless him for going away!
May he be made a duke, and may he also buy a
family seat, so that the Revolution will
know where to look for him at some future day
when it wants him in a hurry; and may ho
always get the advertisement that his soul
craves for, so that the nation may be better
able to identify him when it comes to look for
him.
When the Canadian Premier recently proposed
to despatch delegates to Washington, with
trade reciprocity proposals in view, Secretary
bi.AiNE loftily remarked that he had no objec-
tion to Sir John Macdonald sending over any
number of “ persons ”• to discuss the nn'ual
relations of the two countries, but he coldly
declined to recognise them officially, either as
individuals or as a collective body. He would
not recognise anybody officially as a responsible
negotiator except the British Minister. Here is
a true index of the position of a British colony,
even though it may grandiloquently style itself a
Dominion,” and of the meaning of the term
(( Dependency of Great Britain.” Even an
Australian Federation under the Crown ”
would be cold-shouldered by a practical Ameri-
can Minister, just as France declined recently
to discuss postal treaties with Australian
colonies except through Downing-street. But
the representatives of an “ Australian Republic”
would be received by the American Minister on
terms of equality and with effusive welcome.

When treating of the interminable conflicts
between employers and employed the average
press commentator usually winds up his obser-
vations with the regret that the heated dispu-
tants cannot kiss and be friends “as the in-
terests of capital and labour are in reality
identical.' Now, so long as wages are paid and
profits are in vogue it is simply absurd to insist
that Capital and Labour have interests in com-
mon. ihe prosperity of the capitalist lies in
his success to sell for higher than the cost price
the produce of his workmen. The prosperity
of the wage-slave is determinable by the extent
to which he can reduce the discrepancy between
the cost price of his employers’ goods and the
money that person receives for them in the daily
market. The producer claims that he is
entitled to the whole of the fruits of his
exertions, and as he will never be satisfied till
he gets this and nothing less it is simply bare-
faced misrepresentation to assert that Labour
has anything in common with an artificial
system which compels the worker to pay six-
pence for the privilege of obtaining the self-
same article which he made that day with his
own hands for threepence in order that certain
parasites may have an easy time.
Hear Bulletin, The exodus of working
people and small traders from Maoriland still
continues with unabated vigour. I think there
can be little doubt that the success of the
frozen-meat industry, together with the high
price of wool, sufficiently explain the trouble.
Ihe great sheep-farmers are buying out the
agiiculturists right and left, and farms which
formerly employed scores of husbandmen are
now laid down in grass ; sheep have taken the
places of men. The freezing-works only employ
a few hands compared with the farms, and
wages, since the collapse of the strike, have
>een reduced to ss. a day without board, but
w i tii plenty of broken time, the consequence
being that only grown-up lads and young
bachelors can afford to remain at such work
Add to this the fact that the wealthy sheep-
farmers now import nearly all their own stores
flirect from London or elsewhere and have no
need for local shopkeepers, and the departure
of the trading classes is easily accounted for
tntil some sufficient check is placed on the
monopoly of land and the wholesale laying out
of agricultural lands in grass, or until frozen
mutton and wool come down very materially in
price, Maoriland will continue to retrograde in
everything except the accumulation of wealth
m lew hands.—N.Z. Democrat. Napier N Z

Nevertheless, listening to the old wheezes
that the habitue can foresee ten seconds before

Bland, not content with his fat as the wide-awake
money-lender, pads it out with A idisher slang
most of which on first night was Greek to the

threw at least a third of the circle into convul-

the dough-kneading profession, doesn’t use much
of the potato in compounding his Chicago Irish-
man, who is more closely akin to a Yorkshireman,
but on the whole, a polyglottish sort of party!
In the cabby, H. R. Roberts has a part that would
run alone without a license, quite as good as
Bland s and more easily played. The young actor
doesn t make the most of it, and works it
on exactly similar lines to those of other
parts he- has recently handled. Augustus
Glover plays a haughty patrician of the variety
that fondly imagines no dress-suit is complete with-
out a red silk handkerchief tucked in its shirt-
bosom and Albert Norman's villain is more Italian
than 1 renoh, though not particularly wedded to
any nationality. J. P. West plays Marks, the
would-be virtuous solicitor, whose feet have grown
laige in the hunt for ’oof, as if he were really
aweary of this wicked world. The Violet Chester
of Kate Bishop is sweet and sympathetic, and
v\ alter Howe, the Chester protector, plays with
more fire and ease than usual. Virgy Vivienne
is bright and easy, and all the other people are
fairly good. “London, ’ etc., is a kaleidoscope
in eleven scenes, which fails to clearly carry on
its story, chiefly because its characters are con-
tinually being shaken up before they’ve had time
to settle down. But as it contains only one
murder, no big sensation, few tears, and is full of
funny business, it would probably be popular
for even a longer period than the‘management
will be able to house it. The show’s best features
to our mind, are the scenery and the changes made
with the lights up in full view of the house. In
the first scene, for instance, the flat that forms
the wall of an office falls forward and discloses, on
its hitherto-hidden side, a garden-fence, flower
beds, &c., and, beyond, a rustic, red-tiled cot.
aiu scene, with a view of the
Alhambra, aglow with gas, is also good, but the gem
is a view of the Thames, that is almost too lovely.

It is not generally known whether George
Rignold is a gainer or loser from his propensity
to run everything a week too long at Sydney Her
Majesty’s, but as “The Corsican Brothers ” has
been in hand, after a fashion, for considerably
over twelve months, the public may confidently
expect something gorgeous on Saturday night
next.
*# * *
At Sydney School of Arts, Charles Failing
has seceded for a spell, and the company has been
re-strengthened by the inclusion of Martyn Hagan
and Lucy Fraser, whose patriotic sketch is a dis-
tinctly original and highly popular item. The
Sisters Anderson’s double hornpipe is a lightning
act—one of the fastest terpsichorean goes on
record, and Connie Devereux’s skirt song and
dance “takes well and gives pleasure to all.”
** * *
The success achieved by the Sisters Albu
three Sydney Town-hall concerts has induced
them to give another concert on Saturday night,
when they will be assisted by Messrs. Craven and
T. H. Rainford in soli, duets, and trios. Miss
Clarice Brabazon will contribute pianoforte selec-
tions, Herr Pechotsch will be on hand with his
violin, and Miss Naylor will lend her gracious
presence and technical skill as accompanist. W.
S. Medcalfe manages the season for Mr. Cohen,
who has gone ahead to Melbourne.

At Coogee Aquarium, Manager Larmour
declares that he is weary of the whims and fancies
of pugilists who, “reared in a humpy and on
damper fed,” want the whole Aquarium hung up
for a cronk set-to. In future he intends to do
without them rather than be done by them, as
soon as the Griflo-Murphy match for £lOO purse,
now definitely settled ail but the date, is over.

** * *
Miss Frances Saville, whose portrait
we give elsewhere, and who with her hus-
band, Mr. Max Rown, sailed for Europe
in the Parramatta, is the daughter of
Martin Simonsen, the well-known violinist and
entrepreneur, and Mine. Fanny Simonsen, the
greatest Maritana of the Australian stage.
Francesca Simonsen was born at ’Frisco and
educated at Hamburg. On arriving in Australia
she toured in English, Italian and comic opera,
and finally settled down in Sydney as platform
singer, in 1885. Since then she has been the
soprano of all the principal oratorios, has assisted
Santley, the Halles and other important musi-
cians in their Australian appearances, and was
specially engaged to create the name part in
“Marjorie” during its production in Melbourne.
Mrs. Rown informs us that she intends to hear all
the European vocalists who are worth hearing,
and will probably visit Marches!, ere making any
professional appearance in Europe.

“The Schoolmistress,” having brought
V T ere and the vexing girls overland from Sydney,
re-established her seminary at Melbourne Bijou
un Saturday, when “House full’’was hung out-
side the front-door for the first time these many
weeks. People who remember Minnie Palmer’s
murderous attack upon Pinero’s farce at the
original Bijou will think better of the present
performance than those who now see it for the
first time. The monstrous interpolation of a song
and dance by Peggy and Vere Queckett, in the
second act, used to bring the show into contempt.
By comparison with that music-hall incident the
rehearsal given by Mistress Maggie Moore to the
“comic-oratorio ” composer is first-class business,
albeit the old favourite sang a poor selection of
ditties (including “ Annie Rooney ”) much below
her best form on Saturday. Take it altogether,
however, “ The Schoolmistress ” fell unexpectedly
flat. The natural difficulties which beset
Maggie Moore in the name part were made
especially evident by her nervousness and
newness to her surroundings. Probably she
has never been less at home on any
stage. For the rest, matters are much the
same as in Sydney, the substitution of Annie
Taylor (bright and humorous, as usual) for Miss
Roraer being the one noticeable change. Anson
again works hard to enlarge his small part, Percy
Lyndal and Clitheroe still follow the flag, while
Boucicault and Pattie Browne continue to enjoy
most of the fat. The manager’s conception of
vere Queckett will strike Melbourne folks as being
across between his Beau Farintosh and the Polish
Srinceof “Glass and Fashion.” And those who saw
linnie Palmer play Peggy will surely regret that
Miss Pattie took the Mellikan gal’s exaggerations
so much to heart. She even fixes her hair up in a
ridiculous fashion for no better reason, seemingly,
than that Minnie gave her the idea.

There was a numerous attendance at the
Royal to welcome George Darrell’s “ seventh pro-
duction” of “Transported for Life.” The hand
°f time touches George very gently, especially
about the legs, and as he stands, or sits (George
has a genius for arranging himself on tree-stumps,
®."°-) in the centre of the stage, and breathes
through his revered foghorn, he is exactly the
same as of yore. Neither does his play seem a
day older than it did at its first production, long
ago, for George’s disquisitions on the ways of men,
tunny stage gags and general dramatic effects
were pretty well petrified before he slung them
together. George’s dislike lor the “get-up” of
ordinary humanity seems to grow upon him, and
at whatever moment you drop into the Royal the
spectacle of a vestless hero wrestling with
adversity is right there. But it may be
necessary for George to furnish an everlasting
front view of his shirt. Perhaps his
mighty heart would burst a waistcoat if
he put one on. “ Transported for Life,”
is one of the author’s most aggravating efforts,
insofar that George’s main purpose is to per-

a v 10 l en * ; death, and then to destroy their dream of
happiness by emerging from a shaft-hole, or

proud smile playing round his teeth. Not until
he leads himself to slaughter early in the first act

dramas with gratifying success. Several people

Thanks to Elton and the ballet, “The Old
Guard ” has caught on at the Princess’. On the
tbird nig ] lt litt f e William brought Miss Marie
Keddall forward in her walking costume, to
reeeive the applause due to the designer of a
dance so eminently calculated to drive Melbourne
crazy, and now that clever lady always appears
after her “ royal ballerinas ” have been encored to
exhaustion. It is a pretty dance, telling its own
spicy little story. Some of the ballerinas figure
as boys in satin suits, whilst others are girls in
longish skirts. The orchestra excites the girls to
wild can-can kicks, and lures them on to run
round in a ring, grabbing one another by the ankle
as they go, until a fresh strain of music restores
them to a proper sense of modesty, and makes ’em
feel sorry for having flourished their anatomy in
the air without cons’dering that their mothers
might t approve . So they blush gracefully, and
exit with their faces averted from their natural
enemies. After an encore, it is the boys who pro-
fess to be shocked by what took place, leaving one
to assume that the satin-suited ballerinas are mem-
bers of the Y.M.C.A
out on the loose
Seldom is such a
touching moral lesson
attached to a comic
opera, and the pro-
spect of “The Old
Guard ” running to
great business is,
from a religious
standpoint, extremely
comforting.

“ Falka ” will come to a somewhat sad end
at the Opera House, on Friday, and with it there
will be a strange cutting-short of the Clara
Merivale season. Whether Miss Clara and her
new operas are postponed for a definite or in-
definite period has not yet been announced. Any-
way, the theatre closed on Saturday for a full
rehearsal of “Evangeline,” an “American
romantic burlesque ” of apparent celebrity in its
native land. This novelty will be submitted to
Australian notice on Monday. The composer,
Edward Rice, has come over with six members of
the Yankee company, and if these are as smart as
some of the resident artists—Snazelle, Gourlay,
Forde, Hodson, Fannie Liddiard and others—
engaged to support them, there should be no diffi-
culty in making the romantic burlesque hum.

Carver’s Wild West Show lassooed a few
thousand into the Frb ndly Societies’ Gardens on
Saturday, and the halls are still prospering.
Hopetoun has heaped distinction upon the Wax-
works by going to see that wild delusion, the
Fighting Kangaroo, and the Cyclorama continues
to thrive on the two shilling ticket. But things
are rather dull and lonesome at the Aquarium
since the alligator died, and the Fitzroy neigh-
bourhood wants its Baby-Show badly.
*« * *
“Our Flat,” recently produced at Ade-
laide Theatre Royal, is rather disappointing. The
first act drags and the second is the sole saviour
of the piece which relapses in the concluding act
so badly that you leave “ Our Flat ’’ hurriedly to
seek lodgings more congenial. A young dramatist
who has eloped with his wife to “Our Flat”
writes tragedies with situations “strong enough
to raise hairs on an eggshell,” which are all it
turned. The father-in-law, visiting the flat to sec
his daughter, is mistaken for a money-lender,
with the result that he is persuaded to let his son-
in-law have £l6. Fresh oomplifcatio’ne, hbwevcr„
arise and the author’s furniture is seized for debt,
whereupon his wife, aided by drapery, transforms
old boxes, boards, towel-horse, a hip-bath, cook-
ing utensils, &c., into articles of furniture. She
also writes a farcical comi ily entitled “ Near the
Wind,” which is acceptu t by the managers of the
Star Theatre for £2OO. Then the old man relents,
and takes his children to his arms : Tableau and
curtain. Polly Emery, as the slavey, has the fat
of the piece, and scores every point. As the
dramatic author Robert Inman was natural and
easy. E. W. Royce, wonderfully made up as the
theatrical lessee and owner of an irritating snort,
had but little to do except sit down on the bogus
furniture with unhappy results. Harry Diver, as
the artist, and Edwin Kelly, as the father-in-law,
were all right. Myra Kemble, although imper-
fect in text, was otherwise a satisfactory Mrs.
Sylvester. Business has been big. The Nether-
sole-Cartwright company opened with “The
Idler ” on Wednesday evening.
*slf * *
A private performance of ‘ ‘ The Gondol-
iers,” given at Windsor Castle last month, would
scarcely keep Victoria Regina laughing all the
time. The Republican Royalty which Gilbert
pokes genial fun at is merely one of his topsy-
turvy notions, and the moral which ponderous
“loyalists” have drawn from the court of
Barataria is ridiculously false. On the other
hand there is a sting in many of the librettist’s
radical lines, and genuine satire both in his con-
ception of the Duke of Plaza-Toro (floated into a
limited company for the benefit of Snobocracy)
and his picture of the Duke’s haughty daughters
secretly carrying-on with the family flunkey.
Moreover, the lesson of the opera is not so much
that the Gondoliers are ready to start as re-
publican kings, as that their wives are madly
anxious to sit on a golden throne and put on
“ jam.”
Burdekin says that the N.S.W. Freetrade
Party will fight hard against the Commonwealth
Bill. Quite right. It is designed to murder them.
Cornelius Bourke, crank, aged seventy
something, who kicked his vagrant cell-mate to
death at Hamilton gaol, lias been hanged. The
only moral deducible from Bourke’s execution is
that he had bad luck in being transported from
England to the Vandemonian hell for stealing
six pounds of beef when he was a boy. That’s all.
When standing on the trap he exclaimed, “You
are choking me.” “Yes” replied the hangman,
“ you have just struck it.” At least that’s what
any hangman would have said Avho was reasonably
ready with his tongue.

MISS FRANCES SAVILLE.
Mr. J. J. Doyer, late of the Adrian Hotel,
Amsterdam, has opened a Bodega at Glasgow
Arms, George-street, Sydney, in the Continental
style.
Barrister Costello, whom Judge Harding
put under arrest for five minutes the other
day for “ contempt,” is the man who most deter-
minedly fought against all the prejudices of the
Full Supreme Court, the Law Examiners, the
Law Society, &c., until he forced them to admit
him to the Queensland Bar—a dead set having
been made against him on account of some sup-
posed slur on his moral character relating to the
time when he was in the Education Department.
The judges refused him again and again, but he
came up smiling, and eventually beat all the legal
talent pitted against him.
At Palmerston, Northern Territory, Gov.
Kintore, the overland processionist, distinctly
advocated the employment of yellow and black
labour for tropical agriculture, and berated
Queensland anti-coolie legislation. This is a sam-
ple of the nickel-plated cheek common to out-
siders whose sympathies cannot embrace any
purely Australian sentiment. Who is Governor
Kintore, apart from his reflected plumage of a
Royalty that is daily becoming more distasteful
to the democracy of this continent, that he should
presume to settle the colour question whicli Aus-
tralia has already decided for itself? So long as
Governors confine themselves to windy efforts
born of gaseous wines, and to ornamental func-
tions that tickle the petty vanity of a few silly
peacocks, this democracy can afford to shrug its
broad shoulders with a smile, and await more
useful times ; but when they speak and act as if
they were secret touts and interested agents for
l lie British money-shark and the absentee blood-
leech, The Bulletin’s advice to them is to clear
out of Australia before they are fired out. When
a Kintore can settle the labour question for Aus-
tralia., the GoVernof .-General Will be a nig.
At Poverty Point.

A recent unsuccessful revival of “The Stranger” at
the London Olympic is another reminder that the old
style of play is no good without the old style of players.
O’Grady, who produced “The Famine” at Melbourne
Royal, a few' months ago, has been advertising some
vicious nonsense in London Bra. Amongst other dis-
jointed remarks, he says that “the Firm” supplied him
with a rotten company, and the press was “ paralysed”—
probably because it was for once unanimous in damning
the man s literary effort. As for the support, O’Grady
certainly did tell them at rehearsal that he could get a
better company for £lO a week in dear old England.
Whereupon Clyndes suggested that he ought to hire a
company good enough for hit plays at ten “bob” a week.
But O’Grady doesn’t put that down in the advt. “ for fear
he might forget it.”
Sala seems to have “struck it” this time, for American
papers say—“ The body of the petrified man lately found
under his auspices in Fresno, California, has been sold to
a showman for £2500. Apropos, Sala is said to have
traded off the N.S. Wales marble-man for £l4uo ! When
last heard of, the latter interesting curiosity was in the
possession of Mr. W. F. Buchanan, the opulent squatter,
who didn’t seem overjoyed about the fact. Where is the
“Man” now?
Fuller Maitland, musical critic of London Times, gets
£450 a year and 7s. 6d. for every concert he attends.
Margaret Mather, the actress, recently left her husband
after two or three years of married life. Her reason was:
“ I discovered I’d made a mistake in marrying.” Mar-
garet was free to follow her own dictates, because she can
earn a handsome living for herself. When women can
earn a living as readily as men divorce will be as easy as
marriage.

is no leg-show.

Misled by ill-worded paragraphs in the dailies, The
Bulletin was led to believe that M. Guilmant, the
eminent French organist, was either in, or on bis way to,
Australia. Now, it seems that he is in Paris, and could
not arrive until about the same time as M. Weigand, the
player appointed by the Sydney Council. Under the
circumstances, the Council could not well entertain the
application of M. Guilmant’s agents.
In Charles Arnold’s new play, with which he is stump-
ing the English provinces, he shears a sheep, in full sight
of the audience, inside seven minutes with (we won’t
advertise it) ’s patent shearing-machine. What the
stage wants is a patent for shearing the public or fleecing
boarding-house keepers.
When in Dunedin Johnny Toole bought a photo, of a
Maori man and woman staiiding up to the waist in the
water of the Pink Terrace bath. Then he got photoed.
naked to the waist, stuck the head and shoulders between
the coppery pair, got the lot re-photographed and sent
copies home to Irving and other Hinglish friends.
It is not deemed good business for a fasting-man to be
seen picking his teeth.
