thrown out for dress-circle, the paint-room greatly-
enlarged, dressing-rooms improved, and the stage depth
doubled.
The Indian Planters’ Gazette devotes several columns
to eulogising Mrs. Potter, whom it enthusiastically styles
“ Our Cora.” The old story ; the callow critic is an easy
prey to the artful actress. Manager Twinning is still in
advance.
Barry Sullivan is once more reported dying. Apropot,
we wonder what became of Miss K—?, who went to
England a few days before Sullivan left Melbourne, also
for the old country ? Had old A K—— caught the
distinguished tragedian then, there might have been no
Barry Sullivan for the last 20 years.
A potter, remarks a Bulletin correspondent, would not
think of burning his ware on a sieve with wooden rims,
but Mr. Cartwright, at Melbourne Royal, did so in “The
Middleman,” and Miss Nethersole wears what looks like
the same white dress in three acts in “Moths,” and com-
plains bitterly, in the last act, of the cold, while all the
other actors and actresses are wrapped up in overcoats,
furs, gloves, and top-boots. This is not so bad as the
actor in “Green Lanes of England,” who would persist in
striking bis matches on the sole of his boot in the snow-
storm scene.
Positively the funniest thing ever you heard. The late
King of Prussia and Baron Seidelwitz were desperate
admirers of Cerito, nicknamed Cherrytoes, the danseuse,
and viewed her performances together from a side box,
being so delighted that they got up, shook hands, and
embraced each other ! Hard lines.

Hellmore—Pshaw ! The cigars were to save the

closing all. If lamto be cast forth into the
glitter, J will drag you with me, down, dowh,
down.

the gallely stand it very patiently, after all.

The N.S.W. Ministry deny that any serious
differences exist among them, but the fact remains
that Parkes, chafing under the restraint put upon
his actions by Bruce Smith and M'Millan, who
made his Ministry, and who are not content to be
his creatures, has vowed vengeance in his heart
against the pair.
Pritchard Morgan's new gold-mining
manager in Wales is “Nicky” Williams, an old
Cornish identity of Charters Towers. At one
time Nicky managed the St. Patrick Extended
Block. He is one of those occasional oddities
that mining fields produce—a good, rough, all-
round man, who “done his level best. ” Nicky
shone in the cricket-field as well as in the mine
“ below stairs,” could make a rattling after-dinner
speech, sing a song on call, or stand up and
“maintain his words ” with his “bunch of fives ”
on occasion.
In West Australia no man can go insolvent
unless he owes at least £5O to a creditor, and the
bankruptcy law is so expensive that when he does
go insolvent there is very little left forthe creditor
to receive. For the poor man, it is a harassing
statute; for the big swindler it is a grand cover
for fraud, and lets the fat operator down softly to
the tune of thousands of pounds on a fourpenny
dividend. The small debtor can be worried in the
Local Court for next to nothing, and his first suer
generally sweeps off his goods or lands him in
gaol. West Australia is behind even England in
insolvency barbarisms, running on the old English
Act of 1869.

county.” This innocent little move will entitle him
to play for the club after June, ’92.

tended for circulation in N.S.W. only.
The Census has disclosed a Pitt Town
(N.S.W.) couple—Mr. and Mrs. James Horton—
he 82, and she 89 years ; he a native of Pitt Town,
and she an 80-years’ resident there. James has
never been out of Pitt Town in his life.
Three brothers, two of whom occupy pro-
minent positions in N.S.W., one in the political
and the other in the commercial world, were
known at school respectively as Bully, Brag, and
Sneak. The two former nick-names are still
applicable.

Freiher Senfft Von Pilsach, president-
elect of Apia municipality, passed through
Sydney on his way to Samoa recently. Senfft is
German for mustard, and Von Pilsach, who won
his spurs in Lorraine, will probably make things
hot for the Samoans.

tralia—who in his posthumous Memoirs, challenged
Pitt’s motives for raising banker Smith, from
Nottingham, to the Cariugton Peerage, and said
it was because of pecuniary obligations.
1 jOn the authority of a native of Donegal,
Ireland, it is said that the late Jack Macrossan
was the son of a Donegal doctor. From what we
know of Macrossan personally, we are inclined to
doubt it. .There would at any rate be many
Macrossans in Donegal besides the doctor.
yHow circumscribed is provincial fame !
Sir Henry Ayers is a large, portly, important,
influential, and—er—distinguished citizen of
South Australia. He is even the President of the
Legislative Council of that province, and the
Sydney papers always spell his name “Ayres.”
Stead says he doesn’t believe “that the
Divine Spirit is farmed out to any ring of
ecclesiastical monopolists as if it were a syndicate
with the.exclu-ive conce-sion to a patent medi-
cine.” Nor do we. vVe believe that the Divine
Spirit is farmed out exclusively to Stead himself.
Amongst the successful applicants for
admission to Melbourne Benevolent Asylum on a
recent day, was a Governor of the in titutiou and
anex-M.F. of the colony, Mr. Sydney Ricardo,
at one time a prosperous local agriculturist, who
was the first Victorian to irrigate agricultural
land.
Some of the English papers are still asking
how it is that no inquest was held on the body of
Sculptor Boehm, who died suddenly in the pre-
sence of the Princess Louise, when they were alone
in his studio. The perhaps needless suppression
of enquiry promises in this case to lead to an
historical scandal.
Alfred Deakin, writing of how “India is
governed wholly from without,” declares that
white and native alike are mere ciphers, or
exercise only such illicit influence as is permitted
to women in England and Australia,” Now, who
among Australia’s married politicians are swayed
by illicit influences ?
One of the jurymen who helped to say
No” when they meant to say “Yes” at a
recent Rockhampton trial, is identical with a man
wnO-Qiice sat as Justice to try the case of an
offender against municipal law. The accused
pleaded guilty. “Well, under the circumstances,
I’ll discharge you,” said Dogberry.
Apropos a recent cablegram :
It is a fact which there is no disputing, that aS Sir
Charles and Lady Dilke rode in the Row the other day,
the Prince of Wales cantered up and joined them.
Astonishment, not to say consternation, was depicted on
the faces of many of our “exclusive” set, who had
vigorously “dropped ” Sir Charles, which was not allayed
when the trio turned their horses together for a third
time.
The London correspondent of a Sydney
daily says that “ Her Majesty is deeply annoyed
at the Prince of Wales being mixed up with the
gambling scandal, and she accorded his Royal
Highness a long interview on the this
week, from which the Prince emerged with evident
signs of mental emotion on his countenance.”
What a well-informed correspondent!
Bishop Moorhouse, formerly of Melbourne,
has thrown another bombshell into the circles of
the orthodox. He has practically admitted that
Christ might err with regard to natural science
and historical criticism, Annie Besant impales
the Bishop with Dr. Pusey’s saying, “ If Jesus—
God forgive it!— said One thing that was not
true, divine authority Would be gone.”
Pall Mall.challenges Lord Mayor Savory,
who has been feebly endeavouring to excuse his
unsavory prigging from Spurgeon, “to produce not
only six ideas but any six sentences even in the
whole of his sermon, which are not cribbed from
Spurgeon’s.” The P.M.G. styles the whole
paltry business “an endeavour to eke out civic
grandeur by a spurious show of pious versatility.”
Gilbey, whose name is associated with
gin-labels iu these parts, has become otherwise
famous “at home ” as the founder of several use-
ful horse-breeding societies. In his legitimate
business Gilbey stands alone, being far and away
the largest importer and manufacturer of cheap
wines in the world. His “shilling ” port is-a joke
that passed into the ranks of “chestnuts” 20
years ago, when the stage funny-man could always
fetch a laugh by saying that he would drink some-
body’s healtli m a glass of “ fine old wine—
Gilbey’s, you know.”

We read that Congressman Simpson, who
refuses to wear socks till the duty on worsted is
repealed, and who persistently returns the socks
sent him by the benevolent, was asked by a pretty
woman at the Capitol: “ Is it true that you don’t
wear socks, Mr. Simpson ? Won’t yon let me see,
please?” “Madam,”replied Mr. Simpson,gravely,
“I’m a believer in reciprocity. Do you wear
socks? If you’ll show me yours I’ll show you
mine ! ”
It is said of the late W. B. Dailey that he
was colour-blind, and that when representing
N.S. Wales on his famous emigration lecturing-
tour, with Mr. Henry Parkes, he was dogged an
London by Scotland Yard detectives. His flaring
neckties (generally bright-red or blue) raised the
suspicion that he was one of the “swell mob”
that flourished in the Big Smoke. Australians
always have complained that the status of their
representatives in England is more than doubtful.

The Australasian’ revives a good story
about old Dean Macartney. A temperance meet-
ing was being held in Melbourne Town Hall. The
Dean did not arrive until alter Bishop Moorhouse
(who presided) had confessed himself to be a
mo .mate drinker. Soon after the Bishop sat
down the Dean entered, and when his turn to
speak came he commenced to denounce in his
thorough-going fashion the “moderate drinkers.”
It was this class of persons, he said, that was
responsible for half the drunkenness with which
the city was cursed. The audience simply yelled.

wood. Polling: Hancock, 1682; Wilkins, Hot;

being a Convention delegate) has publicly stated

The enemies of the people ever direct their
notice to external and imaginary foes, French,
Russian, and so on, whereas the foe is within, the
monopcl st, the plural-voter.
It is not generally known that Pulsford
was the candidate supported for East Sydney by
the block vote of the Employers Union. That’s
why he didn’t do much canvassing.

statement that he doesn t want a Republic ; that
lie is “ heart and soul in favour of attachment to
the Crown.” Going for a “ paltry prefix?”

In France, as a result of peasant-proprietor-
ship, out of a population of 37,000,000, o er
24.000. live in the rural districts. In England
and Wales, as a result of the opposite system,
8.000. -only, live in the country, and 17,000,000
in the towns 1

writes to the S, M. Herald to point out that as
Mr. Cotton, the labour-candidate lor East Syd-
ney, was cheered by the same people who hooted
the mention of the Queen’s name, the inference is
obvious. It is—Cotton is obviously no monarch.

Instruction will run with h m in the same interest
for the same constituency at next general election.
Better not. It is a ghastly thing to have a brother
in the ’Ouse, it is as had as being one of five un-
married sisters who look all alike and dress all
alike. -■
What a curious symbol of the decrepitude
Of the British Empire is that Queen’s head, the
Boehm, on its coinage ! It looks all shrinking up,
like water poured on the desert, under the. burn-
ing sun of equality. See how gloriously George
IV. spreads all over the coin, beneath the sun of
privilege.

The breach between Parkes and the people
who took him into “ his ” present Ministry
is widening, and the Convention is aiding the
expansion. With the expiring Parliament there
are lively times coming all round the N.S.W.
and Victorian political arenas—’ol is pregnant
with radical changes.
A member of the Imperial Cabinet has
informed Henniker-Heaton that the question of
the Federation Conference in Sydney was in-
formally mentioned at a recent meeting of the
Government, and that the general opinion among
the Ministers was that the name of “Common
wealth ” was badly chosen and w r as open to objec-
tion.
Thus the Melbourne Argus : “Queensland
Unionism is but the developed type of all labour
organisations, and the appropriate remedy is not
the olive-branch, but the handcuffs,” Let us
paraphrase j “The Argus is but the developed type
of all diseased plutocracies, and the appropriate
remedy is not submission to its grinding domina-
tion, but,the guillotine.” Just as reasonable.
Look at the result of the monstrous plural
Parliamentary-system as exemplified in the
electoral power possessed by the clubs in East
Sydney; Union Club, 434 votes; N.S.W. Club,
105 ; Athenaeum, 7; Australian, 275; Southern,
03 ; Reform, 5 ; and Warrigal Club, 3 ; in all 922
votes possessed by men nearly all of whom have
and exercise votes in other constituencies !
Parkes is going to the country on the
issue “Federation and Freetrade.” Now',
Federation admittedly and inevitably means Pro
tection against the world, and that very quickly.
Ergo, Parkes is going to the country on Freetrade
and Protection. Q.E.D. In this case Freetrade
and Federation are so far unlike the Siamese
twins that life to one means death to the other.
The mission of The People, a new weekly
Democratic sheet, published at Perth, W.A.. is
to effect reform in the present one-sided Conserva-
tive Constitution of that province. The abolition
of the property qualification for members, man-
hood suffrage, and promotion by merit in the
Civil Service will be prominent planks in its
platform. The “ Six Families ” and their friends
have decided not to support the new journal.
The Bulletin stated a few weeks ago that
Sir Henry Parkes was the proposer of “ Common-
wealth ” as a name for the Australian Federation.
That was true. A Melbourne paper now says
that Deakin is in high feather, because he is the
real godfather of the “Commonwealth.” That is
also true. The artful Deakin, knowing Parkes’
weakness, got the term suggested to Parkes, and
(then enthusiastically seconded the Old Man’s
proposal.
A Mr. Begg, of the Imperial Federation
League, remarks that ex-Governor Carington’s
contention that the colonies should be kept free
from restraint by the mother country was not
sound. “It was not reasonable, for instance,
thfit Australia should have unlimited right to
decide from her own standpoint upon questions
which .might involve this country in war.” Just
so; but tfiis argument cuts even more sharply the
other way,
Melbourne Age, by way of explaining that
all public rioting is sinful, recalls to mind the
case of Sir Charles Napier and the Chartists —
“The sympathies of Sir Charles were with the
men in their demands, wholly against them when
.they resorted to violence.” In short, he was
above all things a soldier, having heaps of sym-
pathy for the oppressed until they attempted to
shake off oppression, and then considering tha,t
his duty to the Queep required him to blow away
“ public disaffection ” with a .whiff of grape.
Wholesale Suicide.
The present generation of mankind seems
.determined to proceed along the path that leads
to death —the one that will kill it quickest. The
struggle for existence, business worry, gambling,
horse-racing and all nervous excitements,' tend to
reduce the vital strength and insure early death.
Now-a-days it is the pace that kills ; the body is
.worn out before its natural spell of life is spent.
There is no reason why.all men and women—
physically perfect—should not live" to be centen-
arians, provided care was taken that the system
was not overtaxed in any particular direction.
The symptoms of failing vitality are almost too
well known to need enumeration here; first the
appetite fails, with pain after eating, nervousness
ensiifc, and -f.eepless or restless nights with head-
aches and tired aching limbs on arising in the
morning; a confirmed languor Or lassitude takes
possession of the sufferer, who loses all interest in
pleasure and even in life itself. Unfortunately
such cases are only too common, such experiences
come to every man, and on the first approach of
such sure signs of strength’s faijute, recourse must
be had to a remedy to restore the failing vitality
or quickly he sinks into the everlasting “ for ever-
more.” Such cases, if treated in time, quickly
yield to Clements Tonic. Long standing cases
require protracted treatment, but in all curable
cases Clements Tonic will quickly re-invigorate
the system, restore the vim and spirit of youth,
build up new bone, muscle and nerve tissue, create
an appetite and properly digest and assimilate
food. This is no boast, Clements Tonic is genuine,
that it will do the good its manufacturers claim
for it lias been proved in thousands of cases, as in
.that of W. H. Oram, Esq., Crookwell, N.S.W.,
who writes:—“For nine months I have used
Clements Tonic and have great pleasure in giving
my testimony as to its great efficacy and wonder-
ful curative effects on the nervous system. I have
frequently left the office with severe headache
and despondency, due to excessive mental labour,
have been unable to take any food and had no
appetite, and in every case a dose of Clements
Tonic has quickly removed the melancholy, created
an appetite and caused me to relish a meal. I
have so much faith in Clements Tonic that I can
conscientiously recommend it to professional men
and brain workers generally. I have seen it used
for children with best results, and as for myself I
would not be without it in the house.” And Mr,
Robert A. Luckham, travelling representative of
The Sydney Bulletin, a gentleman known in
almost every city, town and village in Australasia,
testifies to the value of Clements Tonic, thus :
“ Bulletin Office, Sydney,—lt affords me much
pleasure to be able to testify to the many admirable
qualities of Olements Tonic ; having used it for
a short time 1 found my strength wonderfully
restored. I had been suffering from extreme
nervous debility, from which I have now quite
recovered. I consider Clements Tonic a great
tone-giving and delightfully refreshing stimulant.”

WOMEN’S RIGHTS.
Another feature of the (Electoral) bill
be to give women as human beings the rights
to vote. . . . Their liberty is subject to
the laws of the country, and if they commit
a certain class of crimes their lives are sub-
ject to the laws of the country. This being
so, why in the name, of reason should not
they have the power of assisting in' the
making of those laws which they are called
upon to obey ? (Applause.)
At the close ol his address a vote of
thanks was accorded by the audience,
composed chiefly of policemen.

Society
When the Sydney Social Gimlet Brigade
failed upon Parkes with the mild request that
he would hand all the giddy pictures in the
cigar shop windows Over to Nosey Bob, the liailg-
man, to be burnt, a good brother (possibly know-
ing Sir ’Enry’s aversion) drew his attention to ohe
particularly offensive specimen. “Ydu’ll hob-
serve,’ he said, “the extreme hindelicacy Of the
production, Sir ’Enry, when I hexplain that at
first sight it’s perfectly chaste, and that in order
to see the thing in all its vileness you’ve got to
turn it hup.side down, and then (holding it at
arm’s length) to look at it steadily with one heye
shut.” It’s surprising what aptitude some zealous
Christians have for turning divine.sunbeams round
pasty corners. Bishop Barry who was one of the
deputation; asked the reporters to omit all mention
of the good man’s ingenuity, and the obedient
papers did. You should just hear Parkes tell this
story.
* * *
A (two-ounce) glove exhibition (not prize-
fight that would be low) for money came off a
little while back at a fashionable Melbourne
athletic hall. At the end of the sixth round one
of the lads was in a fearful state —his nose was
smashed clean across his face, part of one ear was
hanging and part of it was off (’twas afterwards
stitched on), both eyes were black, and the boy’s
body was a mass of br .ises. He was, indeed, a
sorry spectacle. But the gentlemen of the club
(among them some of the leading doctors, law-
yers, &c.) howled for another round, and at the
end thereof there was a general stampede for hats
and overcoats as a very limp figure was carried
out, placed in a cab, and driven to Dr. Fizgig’s
hospital. For some days afterwards not a°soul
had seen the fight - oh, no. A cheque for £3O was
handed over to the battered one, who ultimately
Went for a trip, and the gentlemen breathed
freely again.
* * *
Bob Ram age, the great Carbine’s
jockey, has had a daughter born unto him. Had
it been a son and heir it would, in all probability
have been called Carbine ; but, being a girl it has
been decided to call her Mary Mersev (Carbine’s
dam) Ramage.
* # #
John Kruse, the young Australian-born
violinist, is, we read, shortly to be married to an
Austrian Countess. If John is yoking himself to
the nobility let him beware of re-entering a vulgar
country where the mention of the name of Kruse
recalls, not only melody, but also bug-powder.
Insecfcide is unstable regarded as a foundation
for romance. Apropos, we remember an Italian
Countess who ca-me out to Australia to marry a
pompous gumsucker with castor-oil on his
curls, who had met her while travelling in Italy
and had told her that his family lived in a splendid
Australian palace of which he showed her a
photograph. On arrival she found that he did
live in a palace. It was the State Treasury, and
the young man’s father, as messenger, had
quarters therein. Then the Italian Countess
went back to her maccaroni, priests and bad
street smells.

As they have altered the name of the
Yarrangohilly (N.S.W.) Caves to the “Jersey”
Caves, let us hope that when next Governor Jersey
has anyone to christen he will return the com pin
ment by naming him or her “ Yarrangobilly
Villiers.” This, in the immortal words of Alfred
Bennett, “would take well and give pleasure to
all.”

. party of Melbourne nobilities recently
visited the Gipsland Lakes’ entrance. Amongst
them were a Cabinet Minister (married) and a
well-known sculptor and his wife, a very beautiful
dame. The sculptor missed the train at one of
the stations, having outstayed his allotted interval
for alighting. A young joker, who was also of the
party, at the next stopp ng-pli.ce, sent a wire back

A Maoriland bankrupt’s record :—First
insolvency, offered his creditors his own unse-
cured bids for 12 months--20s. in the £ with 10
p.c. added; accepted. Bills matured and were
dishonored ; bankrupted afresh. Second insol-
vency for total phis 10 p.c.; offered his creditors
15s. in the £, with 10 p.c. added. Bills matured
and were dishonored; once more into the mill,
dear friends. Third insolvency for 75 p.c. of
“total plus 10 p.c.,” with 10 p.c. added on the
75 ; offered his creditors 10s. in the £, with 10 p.c.
added. Why detail ? Bung again, of course, and
got off with ss. in the new total of debts, bn
unsecured bills at 12 months. Not a bill was paid.
Off-colour once more, and offered Is. in the £
with usual interest; small insolvency this time if
you calculate it, and as he was a small man they
made him find security. This time every bill was
“honorably paid,” and the honest man stands
accredited before the world as a reputable trader.
Work it out and you will find that for every £lOO
ho owed at first, he paid rather less than 15s 6fd.
and rather more than 15s. Grand financing,
this.

Some members of the band of the N.S.W.
Naval Artillery Volunteers when dismissed the
service by the authorities because they played dis-
cordantly at the officers’ mess in revenge for
having been refused leave of absence for the pur-
pose of playing elsewhere for money, went off
from the encampment in a bum-boat and blared
defiance with bassoons at their cruel oppressors
to the great amusement of a big crowd.

Hawk Moore is not surprised to hear that
Mr. Edward Bulwer Dickens is not a success in
the Sydney Legislature, to which he was returned
last year, and hears that a recent speech of his
was received with derisive cries. “The N.S.W.
Assembly is a very peculiar body, holding refine -
nient in contempt, and admitting only one claim
to respect—descent from the original colonists .
Botany Bay,” says Moore. Not so. Mr. E. B. L.
Dickens was elected chiefly because lie was the
son of his father—because there still lingers in the
N.S. W. electors some faint traces of respect for
the absurd system of hereditary honors, to main-
tain wtiich in England appears the chief end and
aim of Mr. Disgustus Moore and his assistant
boot-lickers.

* # #
A partner in one of the largest wholesale
houses in Australianowholds that silence is golden.
Some time ago he paid dearly for learning how to
stick his tongue in his cheek. It was like this.
The commercial-room likes to spice its drinks
with scandal, and not to be behind-hand, our
little friend of “ the roads ” trotted out a skeleton
from the local surgeon’s cupboard. Unluckily
for him, the medico himself was amongst the
audience, and when the story was ended not only
complimented the bagman on the veracity of his
yarn, but proceeded to add an element of humour
—hoisting him on to the dinner-table, partially
disrobing him after the fashion adopted by stern
fathers, and, much to the amusement of the com-
pany, administering a sound caning. The bagmen
of S. A. know this yarn.

# * *
Lieut. Vivian Tozer, J.P., the longshank
young son of Queensland Minister for War Tozer,
and now himself commanding a squad of 11 men
and devastating the back lanes of Isisford, the
village of “Fruit Salt Jump-up,” will not cost
the country much for forage tf he confines his
calls on the commissariat to “Welsh rare-bits.”
Viv. was once up en tour with his dad, when he
saw grand hotel life for the first time, and
luncheoned tahle-d'hote. On the bill-of-fare
was “ Welsh rabbit.” When the august waiter
came for his order, Viv. said meekly—“l’m very
fond of Welsh rabbit; I’ll have some. ” “Yessir
’ow many ? ” “ How many ? Oh, half a little one
will do.” “Yessir,” said the waiter, and never
turned a hair, but brought it. Yiv, was too de-
pressed to make any remark about the small
square of yellow leather on toast, and that’s all he
took for dinner that day. If any shearer yells
out at Tozer, “How do you like Welsh rabbit?”
lie’ll be run in for certain.
# * «
The first two weddings at the new St.
Paul’s Cathedral, Melbourne, took place last week.
The first one was of “gentry, but poor”—that is
to say, not wallowing in lucre, such as the Church
delights to honor. So the service was “slum-
mocked,” mumbo-jumboed over anyhow. But
now came the Golden Calves, the Holy Yeal—
oho ! and the sacred fane was desecrated by
restricting admission to holders of gilt-edged
cards, and Toorakia came prancing along. Extra
parsons rolled up~Rev. Stretch appeared in his
stole as wonderful as Joseph’s coat of many
colours. Rev. So and So assisted, also Rev. So
and- So. The service was most impressively
chanted, the organ was unlocked, and forth oame
the delicious Wedding March and Voice that
breathed o’er Eden, for Adam and Eve in golden
fig leaves, with ivory-backed prayer-books. And
lo ! a sweet savour ascended, and the Most High
called for an ounce of civet, good apothecary !
Apropos, Rev, Hugh Price-Hughes says: “Mil-
lionaires must go.” “By jingo !” sarcastically
remarks Carnegie, “if the millionaire goes, he
takes tile parson with him. They will go forth
together hand in hand, l>ke Adam and Eve out
of Paradise, seeking light employment, with
heavy pay.”

was a bo/ he formed a romantic acquaintance
with Lord Palmerston. Tins is a pity, because
the story’s good enough for Miss Braddon. Mak-
ing up his mind One day to see the world—so says
the —he shipped as supercargo on board a
vessel returning to Londbn, and, shortly before
Sailing, fell in with a poor devil on the wharf, Who
OHe red him a letter to Lord Palmerston. Jack
thought it a practical joke. At all eVents, the
letter was carried in due time to the official
address, and, heeding the warning that he was to
deliver it personally, the young adventurer ran
the gauntlet of messengers up to the door of the
great Foreign Secretary’s room. The Irig man
stroked his hair (not so white in those days) and
invited him to Broadlands for a little sport. The
finest bird they winged was a beautiful woman.
1 hey found her soon after breakfast sitting in a
very snug nest within the grounds of the ances-
tral mansion. “This is the young gentleman
Who brought me the letter from Australia,” said
Pam. The lady r —a dignified, middle-aged party rtf
the Virgin Queen type—WAs interested in 'the
youthful visitor. The youthful visitor, it is said.
Was quite as interested in the striking resemblance
she bore to the young man loading ships with
hides and tallow on the wharves of Port Jacksoh.
As old Jack denies the Story, of course he did hot
speculate upon the relationship of the Mihistet of

The Federation delegates have been feted
and lavishly treated at the expense of N.S. Wales,
but it is alleged that not one of them has had the
ordinary decency to give even a sixpence in return
for the services of the waiters and messengers who
have been at their beck and call during the long
and dreary hours of the Convention gathering.
* * *
A “tragedy” report in Melbourne Aue
mentions that “Mr. Joseph Coyne, a labourer, at
work near the spot, heard two shots fired.” Good.
An English paper, always careful of social dis-
tinctions, would speak of —“ a labouring man,
named Joseph Coyne.” The point is worth
noticing.
* * #
Some of the little darlings who danced in
an amateur ballet at the People’s Palace, Mel-
bourne, in a church-aid show, threw up their legs
to such a giddy height that even their mammas
were shocked. Two or three parsons, however,
watched this novel entertainment without shud-
dering. At other times, too, these broad-
principled shepherds might have been seen
gloating over the antics of a professional coryphee
—a Mdlle. Somebody—whose skirts were con-
siderably shorter than the average sermon. The
Church evidently regards legs as “innocent,
rational amusements,” so long as they are assist-
ing to build a new Sunday-school, or reducing the
organ debt. And the better the leg, the better
the deed.

* * *
The death of George Harris, of Newstead,
Brisbane, closes one of the most hospitable and
popular houses in that city. The old man is said
to have arrived in Bananaland, from England, at
the age of two years, and to have been 60 when he
died, but years are apt to get mixed up in young
countries, and the writer, at any rate, would fancy
George Harris much older than 60, and with a
previous knowledge of other parts of Australia.
However, J. and G. Harris were established as
merchants in Brisbane in its early days, and were
followed up by George Harris and Co., the
“ style ” of the deceased subject of these remarks.
He had social and financial fancies of a very broad
kind, though never accused of possessing too much
money. One of his daughters married R. G.
Casey, of Normanby Station, and the other G. C.
Taylor, of Bellevue, Esk district. The latter was
the son of Millionaire Taylor, “ king of
Toowoomba,” and ectype of N.S. Wales’ own
Jack Robertson as a picturesque orator and
“ bonvive. ” The old man himself married a Miss
Thorn, said (like the late Mrs. Donald Wallace)
to have been born under the tilt of a bullock-
dray, and one of the most beautiful girls ever
born anywhere. She developed into a kind,
matronly hostess, and Newstead was the social
refuge of every wandering and homeless English-
FATTENING FATTA.
Sing a song of Fatta GHarid,
Wretched heathen sinner j,
Got to hang, you understand;
But ignored his dinner.
Not the former, but the latter
Trouble seemed to be the matter,
For, instead of growing fatter,
T atta dwindled thinner.
Sing a song of Eatta Chand—
Gruesome little ballad--
Beef he wouldn’t look at, and
Chucked away his salad.
Fanciful as any wench is
Who objects to Melbourne stenches,
He was getting what, in French, is
Recognised as mal- ade.
Sing a song of Fatta Chand.
Doctor kept on banging
Of his forehead with his hand,
While he stood haranguing
All day long that naughty nigger,
Who was losing of his figger
Which they wanted plumper, bigger,
Heavier for hanging.
Sing a song of Fatta Chand,
Sullen and dejected.
Fresh arrangements have been planned
Food is now injected.
Nothing’s put upon his platter,
Yet they load him up with batter.
Fitter now is brother Fatta
■ Than the Crown expected.
■Sing a song of Fatta Chand,
Though with Fate he wrangles,
Soon he’ll join a Hindoo band
Up am ng the “angles,”
Blessings on the good physician
Who fulfils his legal mission
To get Fatta in condition,
Ere Ins body dangles.

* * *
The Bulletin warns whom it may concern
of a new style of swindle. It happened in Bris-
bane a few weeks back and is suspected of being
tried in Sydney now* Advertisements are pub-
lished calling for tenders. Applicants turn up in
numbers at the ostensible architect’s office, inspect
plans, &cv* put in tenders, and call again. Each
is asked to make a deposit as security, and a day
is named for acceptance of one tender and the
return of the remaining deposits. That day is
really the Judgment Day. When the specified
date arrives, the office is closed and the architect
is found to be “a stranger, who took the office a
few days before and has left.” Moral -. Deal only
with known local men, and give guarantees but
not cash deposits.
# * #
At Walcha (N.S.W.) Show the high jump
for lady riders resolved itself into a match
between Mr. Jas. Moor's Pine Tree (Miss N.
Fletcher) and Mr. Richard Doaks’ Joker (Mrs.
Stace). They continued to top the bar till it was
raised to oft. Gin.', which both horses cleared in
splendid style. The committee then stepped in
and stopped the proceedings, although the riders
wished to go on to a finish. Miss Fletcher, we
read, is a native of Walcha, and Mrs. Stace of the
Upper Hastings.

# * *
It is a melancholy satire on London
journalism that the World should advise Truth
to procure another editor, “one more conversant
with the ways of clubs and society.” What lofty
qualifications for an editor ! Truth’s reply to
this will probably take the form of a sneer at the
World for having issued a “ridiculously in-
accurate” account of what the Queen did after
dinner last Thursday three weeks, or a crushing
denial of the report published in our contemporary
respecting the Duke of Connaught’s arrangements
for the third Saturday in December.

# # #
The only actually offensive things in the
Melbourne Art Gallery are the colossal sitting
statues of Victoria, Albert, Wales* and Alexandra,
by Summers, sloppy, hastily-sculped produc-
tions, entirely unworthy of that artist genius.
But with what disgust he did them, to the order
of Bart. Clarke, who vainly hoped for a peerage !
Summers, while in Melbourne many years ago,
did a figure of Albion, over a drapery-shop, in
Richmond, and also sculped the cherubs and gar-
lands along the frieze of the Bank of New South
W ales.
# * X
Ada Christina Reid, of Tintinhull, N.S. \V.,
has had her broken heart half-soled at Tamworth
Circuit Court, at which she impeached her
faithless lover, Walter Scott Laidlaw, a mounted-
constable, for breach of promise. It was a
rather prosaic affair. The parties had known
eacli other from childhood, and Walter had been
faithful until change of scene altered him. He
was foolish enough to write Ada an offer
of marriage, and also to write asking
her to forget him, as he had been de-
partmentally “ reported,” and his official
career was blasted and he didn’t want her to share
his misery. Ada asked the Court to award her
£5OO, as she had refused an interim offer from “ a
gentleman of good repute,” and had fallen through
grief “into a bad state of health.” “The plain-
tiff,” meltmgly exclaimed Judge Windeyer, “is
thrown upon the marriage-market as a damaged
article.” Verdict for £lOO.

Queer how things turn out ! The wife of
one of the Australian labour leaders, when some-
thing under two years old, nearly died for want of
food and water through the brutality of a Queens-
land squatter who, in a time of drought, wilfully
directed her father thirty miles out of his road to
prevent him driving through his run. The young
lady is said to have demolished a bottleful of
pickles en route. Her husband is now distributing
the Vinegar.
Everything lias its uses. Bustles having
gone out dog-muzzles seem to be coining in, even
in hydrophobia-less Australia.

a preventive for embezzlement. It does not con-

betray symptoms of over-godliness, but in “the
maintenance of a stringency of supervision and

general manner of life of officers in other words,

Premier Parkes has written to Dilke saying
that he believes him perfectly innocent* To the
pure, All things are pure.
Anthony Ogdon is to be Labour-candidate
at Townsville election. W. V. Brown reckoned
he would have a walk-over.
It is cabled that “The date of the marriage
between Mr. G* S. Parnell and Mrs. O’Shea has
been fixed ” by the daily papers ?
Judged from his public utterances, Sir
George Grey’s ideas as to the Single-tax are
exactly on all-fours with those of The Bulletin.
Sir John Macdonald, tile Parkes of Canada,
says “a Briton he was born and a Briton he will
die.” He ought to die A Briton, as sooh as
possible*
The Victorian Minister for Education has
decided that no State-school teacher may give, at
any time, religions instruction to any person ill
any State-school.
J. C* Bray, S*A. Chief Secretary, is the
son of a shoemaker, and is a comparatively self-
made man. Premier Play ford is handicapped by
being a parson’s son*
John Bates, Who landed on Kangaroo
Island (S*A.) over 10 years before South Aus-
tralia was proclaimed, reached his 91st birthday
last week* and is still hale and hearty.

BUGLER; “Yes, sir; I see’d her walking back ’ome wi’ the

Sir John Morphett, who was present at
ex-Governor Grey’s reception in Adelaide, is the
only surviving member of the first S. A. Legisla-
tive Council, which was constituted by Sir George.
M ary McCarthy, 106 years of age, and
born 30 years before Waterloo, .died in Adelaide
District Asylum last week. She was the only
centenarian who didn’t claim to have fought at
Balaklava.
Sir George Grey thinks that South Aus-
tralia will continue to lead the world in demo-
cratic legislation, and he considers it remarkable
that such a small city as Adelaide should contain
so many brilliant men.
Bismarck’s entry of the Reichstag as
elected member is even more significant than his
fall. It is a come down from his pedestal, entry
to grapple in the arena like a mere Gladstone or
Gambetta. This revolutionises the German Par-
liament.
Freetrade is badly handled in the ruction
all over the globe. United States, Canada,
France, Germany, protectionising as hard as they
can, while Adam Smith, Bastiat, and all that
school are flung in a corner, because they over-
looked the need of National Development.
When the Czarewitch and suite were
seated at a banquet given them in Madras lately
a bottle of sodawater on the verandah burst with
a loud report; whereupon the Heir-Apparent of
the Russian Empire started to his feet and drew
his sword half out of the scabbard, prepared for
the worst.
Premier Munro told his Geelong constitu-
ents that Victoria may welcome the Federal Con-
stitution as the greatest blessing which could he
offered. Geelong will put in its pious little cl im
to the position of Federal city, on the ground that
no place is better fitted to save the soul of a Com-,
monwealth.
The return of Trades Hall Hancock at the
top of the poll for Collingwood is a real “ facer ”
for the Age, and makes, people ask where the
political influence of that organ comes in. The
Melbourne working men read no other daily
paper, and they invariably vote just as David
Symo tells them not to.

“Union to the Core.”
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
We are shearers, and not wealthy ;
We haVe little in our store;
But our hearts are true and healthy*
We are Union to the core.
Now, the Saviour's Twelve Apostles
Found a Blackleg in their crew;
So, when we first came to jostles,
We found Blacklegs with us, too*
Chorus.—But let Scabs and Blacklegs falter,
And let Squatters’ Gatlings roar*
True to Truth* m cannot altet—
We are Union to the core.
WheH “The Cove ’’ got round some fellows,
We implored ’em, there and then,
Not to show white-liver’d bellows*
But to act like sterling mem
Then one said —“ It’s easy blowing*
But our sugar’s fairly spent,
So we must work, if it’s going,
For we’re stumped of every cent.”
Chorus.—But let Scabs and Blacklegs, &h.
Tlieh fve tossed along our “ pooches,”
And we swore to see ’em through ;
And they meekly turned, like “moocheyS* ’
And behind the sogers dretv.
Once again we passed the forces,
Chancing all to save ’em* swore —
“ Why, we’ll 'give you up oUr horses!
Can a bushrnan offer more ?”
Chorus.—But let Scabs and Blacklegs* &c*
As a nigger loVes his “gunny*”
As a bookie loves the course*
As a miser loves his money,
So a bushrnan loves his horse !
Yet, to help the Union Forces
(They have always had our store)*
Why, we’d even give oUr horses I
For we’re Union to the core.
Chors. —Then* let Scabs and Blacklegs falter*
And let Squatters’ Gatlings roar,
Horse and man* we’ll never alter*
Both are Union to the core.
THE DIPSOMANIAC.
What is it?
THE INFLUENCE THAT SOMETIMES GUIDES US
AGAINST OUR WILL.
“No, I do not believe in ghosts,” said an old
lady of great experience, “ but I do believe in the
ministration of spirits.” This is not the age nor
the country in which the supernatural flourishes!
Interest centres about the practical. Haunted
houses are rented without great difficulty, if the
landlord is willing to knock off a portion of the
rent. There are many, however, who will admit
the exertion at times of some peculiar influences
swaying our actions. We are led to do things we
should not do of our own volition—the wisdom of
•which we do not appreciate until afterwards;
then we wonder how we came to do them. We
are forced to learn that worldly wisdom is not
always that which guides into the wisest courses.
In tliis practical age remits are what people are
looking after. They believe in what they see;
the'“ evidence of things not seen ” is to be
weighed in the future. When a man is sick he
wants to get well, he caves little what medical
method is employed if it cures. But if it does not
cure he soon begins to doubt the skill of the
physician, and enquire into his methods of medi-
cation. A happy thing it is sometimes if some
influence induces investigation before it is too
late. Mr. E. W. Edmondson, of Melbourne,
writes, 21st October, 1890: —“ During the past 10
years I have been ailing periodically from a
serious affection of the kidneys, and in latter
years my complaint became more acute, so that I
was frequently seized with stabbing pains in the
back. 1 had often prolonged headaches, the
result of liver disorder. For years I thus suffered,
and I thought this disorder of the kidneys and
iiver would bring about a more serious disease.
Finally I tried the affects of Warner’s safe cure,
and not until then did I experience any ameliora-
tion in my condition. The use of this medicine
speedily wrought a great change and four bottles
effected a complete cure.” Note what Mr. Henry
Warwick, of Lonsdale Street, Melbourne, says:—
“ 30th October, 1890 —For some years I have been
suffering from frequent attacks of gravel, often
with unbearable pains in the back and right side,
and my digestive organs were also out of order.
In addition 1 was afflicted with congest on of the
liver and periodical headaches of a severe nature.
My suffering was quickly removed when re-
sorting to Warner’s safe cure, of which I took four
bottles, when the acute pains in the right side,
caused by the congestion of the liver, ceased,
while my kidneys were restored to healthy action.”

846 Maoquarie-slreet North, Sydney, April 22, 1891.
My Dear Moorabinda.—
If I were going to marry some wealthy old man do you
think I’d be so foolish as some girls are over the allow-
ance question ? They are so terrified that he might think
they marry him for money, that when he says (as happened
lately), “ We’ll arrange your allowance when we’re mar-
ried, ray dear,” the poor things acquiesce ecstatically, and,
if they are very artful, throw in, perhaps, some fathom-
less assertion that “all gold is dross beside a loving
heart,” &c., &c. In Victoria recently a young woman
became an old man’s darling simply for the accruing cash,
but when the orange-blossoms had been swept into the
corner and the lady was cold “Mrs. 5.,” she found that
30s. a week was her pin-money, and that all bills had to
be submitted to the master for payment ! Very few rich
matrons have as much money as people think they have,
and it is a well-known fact that one “rich” Australian
woman actually can’t get a threepenny-bit without asking
for it, and never carries a purse ! This is one way in
which Robin Gray actually gets square on the deal and
Jeannie is paid out for her double-shuffle.
I wonder why caterers for big ball-suppers don’t label
the principal sweets, just as they do the sandwiches and
sherry? At public balls especially this is a long-felt
want, for the ignorant of both sexes usually indicate
their fancy by asking for some of that “shaky pink
thing”(or as the case may be), while Jeames curls his
long upper-lip in professional contempt. Then it would
save the unaccustomed young man from being airily dis-
patched for Charlotte Russe and coming back with tipsy-
cake, and having to blacken his soul by declaring that
Charlotte was off, with his conscience telling him all the
time that he didn’t know even what she was made of
Labelling all the big dishes would lift a great weight
from the minds of the young men who fetch and carry
for hungry partners.
Mr. and Mrs. Burdekin have now become a myth to
Sydneyites (some people say that they were that all along
to some of the citizens), and the Manning regime is in-
augurated in the usual manner, amid the widespread
hope that the new order of things civic will essentially
differ from that of the Burdekin era, when Society was
first and the “City” nowhere. The ex-Mayor and
Mayoress plainly showed their hand over their first and
only ball, when people who, with clock-like regularity,
had attended the Mayor’s ball from year to year were
scratched off the invitation-list and the cards issued on
a strictly “ select ” basis. The Burdekins certainly enter-
tained a great deal, but not the kind of people who
expected to be entertained, and in consequence they w T ere
(in a purely civic sense) about as unpopular a couple as
Sydney has ever seen. Pretty Mrs. Burdekin was cer-
tainly the most charming Mayoress we have ever had—
only the bulk of her “ subjects ” never got an opportunity
to find it out.
They say that the reason why an emotional married
actress was not invited to join the Austral Salon was that
somebody saw her gallivanting about Melbourne Botanical
Gardens at an early hour “ with one of the company,”
who had his arm round her waist, while her lovely head
rested on his shoulder and she fanned him gently with a
palm-leaf screen. It was a graceful tableau, and it only
the other party had been her husband-but hold! Per-
haps in Melbourne, as in Sydney, the authorities object
to a man hugging his own wife in the Gardens. We all
know what happened when Curator Moore, of Sydney
Botanical Gardens, caught a man nursing his (the man’s)
wife.
Melbourne’s brand-new Cathedral, St. Paul’s, solem-
nized-its first wedding the other day, Dr. William
Chisholm, the accomplished young surgeon, of Macquarie*
street north, Sydney, and Miss “Pinkie” Mitchell, third
daughter of Mr. T. Mitchell, of Bringenbrong, Upper
Murray, N.S.W. (formerly of Victoria), being the
culprits. The ceremony was very quiet, although the
floriture for a “grand” wedding which came later gave
quite an elaborate air to the bell, book and candl e
business ; and after the knot had been irrevocably tied a
huge family Bible was given to the happy pair as a
souvenir of being the first couple united on the premises.
The second wedding (meant to be the first) on the same
day at the same cathedral, was that of Miss Thirza
Aitken, daughter of Mr. Thomas Aitken, of “ The Elms,”
East Melbourne, to Doctor Herman Lawrence, of Victoria.
•Che service (fully choral under the gifted direction of
Organist Wood) cost £5O, and the whole affair was
gorgeous, there being eight bridesmaids and two pages in
white satin and lace, while the pretty bride wore % hand-
some gown of white Duchesse, brocade and chiffon and
carried a nosegay of white exotics and ferns. The wed-
ding-ring was a slight departure from the conventional
plain gold band, having “LOVE” engraved on it. On
her marriage-day the bride came into a fortune of forty
thousand pounds. It is rather a coincidence that of the
two initial marriages at St. Paul’s both brides were third
■daughters, and both bridegrooms medical men.
The weary old cry is going up in the newspapers over
the lady-help question—the beggarly pittance for work
that reaches from the parlour to the dust-box. An advt.
appears in a Sydney daily for a lady-help to do everything
under the sun—from playing the piano to scrubbing
floors—for £25 per, annum—los. a week! How the
niggardly housewife must bless that shadowy fetish,
‘Appearances!” which induces a young woman, who
might bo a skilled domestic at a good wage, to dress
jmatly, be ladylike, do the housework all day and enter,
tain guests after she has washed up at night, on a pittance
that Bridget would sneer at! And what is the advan-
tage? I have seen “Miss Jones” on the field of her
duties—have seen the vulgar look of contempt the “lady’>
visitor throws at her worn finger-nails and knocked-about
hands as she essays to accompany her song-have seen
how she Sits, almost unnoticed, unless she happens to be
pretty, and then the young men think it their privilege
to treat her as I have seen them treat waitresses. And
these, remember, are the “ advantages” of her situation.
Now that Lady Jersey has arisen and responded “ ably
and fluently” to the toast of “The Ladies,” at Maitland
Show banquet, we will no doubt have a rest from the sight
of the fat, hazy M.P., steadying himself on the table and
stating the old mournful poser: “Shentle- (hie)- men,
where, I say, where would we have bin if it hadn’t been
for the ladies?” In his stead some bewchus and bilious-
looking being with the gift of the gab will arise with the
grace of an angel in course of resurrection and, calm in
the knowledge of vice-regal precedent, will cackle lovely
woman’s acknowledgements—probably until thechairman
orders her to sit down. But, for myself, I would much
rather have a man stand up for the ladies, because (to
advance a painfully frivolous reason) women live only to
be flattered, and it is so nice to hear high-flown compli-
ments even though you know that they are insincere. A
woman in “reply” couldn’t very well be flattering, since
it is the worst of taste to draw attention to your own
virtues.
From Mr. George Castledon, of Newcastle (N.S.W.), I
have received a very danceablenaltz called the “Mignon”
with a good swing in it from first to last, without which a
waltz might just as well be an Irish jig. It is easy to
play and could be learnt by memory (as all dance-music
must be) almost in one sitting. Altogether the “Mignon”
valse ought to have quite a run at drawing-room dances,
carpet “at homes,” and other haunts of the amateur
musician.
After all, there is nothing like calling a spade a spade.
Says a recent Sydney advt. :
A THOROUGHLY EXPERIENCED Housekeeper
_ seeks ENGAGEMENT to widower, children not
objected to. Address Experience, Post-office, Wool-

A registry-office woman once told me that one of the list
of her household guardian angels confessed, in a burst of
confidence, that she always carried a white silk “that
would do for a wedding-dress ” in her trunk whenever she
undertook the responsibility of looking after the creature
comforts of a bachelor or a widower.
Some physiologist, apparently a woman, has discovered
that a woman should weigh two pounds to every longi.
tudinal inch, and that the average woman—sft. fin. with-
out shoes—should weigh 128 to 130 pounds ; her waist
should be 25in., her chest from back to front 6in., and
her bust 30 to 31 inches. These are ideal measurements,
of course—what women ought to be, not what they are ;
for, upon the propounder of these weights and measures
examining 325 average young women, it was found that the
distance round the body, from front to back, was painfully
defective in most; it was also found that the average
waist was 22, and bust 29. The theory of the properly-
proportioned woman weighing two pounds to the inch is a
regular throw-in for the sylph of sft. 10in., who is perfectly
justified in turning the scale at lOst.
A Melbourne lady dress-reporter had a dreadful experi-
ence, the other day, at a big Jewish wedding. She
wanted to browse round among the gifts and tick off the
names of the people who had given tea-pots and things,
but when she sought to enter this plated Paradise her
way was barred by a member of the family: “You can’t
see the presents now, there is no one to show them to
you.” “Oh, I don’t want anybody—l suppose the cards
are attached?” And then came the answer ; “Oh, cer-
tainly, but do you think we’re going to trust you with the
presents alone ?” To her credit, be it said, the Lady
Jenkins turned round immediately and left the house.
At a big, swell ball given in Sydney, the other evening,
one of the debutantes was heavily powdered from her
shoulders to her forehead. What a lovely work of art
she will be when she has been going for one or two
seasons !
In the S. M. Herald, the other day, a waif crying out
for bread and lodgings ended his advt.—“ Grown-up
daughters objected to.” Poor young man ! I suppose
the iron must have entered his soul in the usual way.
First, the beauteous Being who looked really dashing
when the curling-papers were off and who played his
accompaniments at an angle of ten degrees towards him
and who was so fond of sitting in the gloaming “Aren’t
you, Mr. Smythe?” second, came the breach of promise
case when the lady fainted in the witness-box and her
mother shook her umbrella at him. Or perhaps he has
suffered at the hands of the domesticated daughter who
would make him all his favourite dishes and send him his
breakfast in bed—until the other boarder came.
Yours affectionately,
SAPPHO SMITH.

This German custom of the second-ballot
is good, requiring the winner to have an absolute
majority. In Australia it would have got us
Labour members long ago. At the same time it
would have nipped out Hancock, in Gollingwood
last week.
Special Reporter Lambie of Melbourne
Age, (who went to the Soudan for the S. M.
Herald) was mistaken for a squatter’s spy at
Barcaldine shearers’ camp the other day, but
managed cleverly to explain his identity and mis-
sion ere a few excited men carried out a notion of
washing him under the bore-tap.
The venerable Sir George Grey,—he was
80 on 14th instant—had not been in Adelaide two
hours ere he, after the public reception was over,
went to visit the cemetery containing the grave of
his only child, who died over 45 years ago. The
mother, who has long since passed away, was,
when Grey married her, a beautiful girl of 16
years—the daughter of a Government official at
Albany, W.A.
The London police are making raids on
betting “clubs” agam. They do these things
every fifteen years, or so, and when half a dozen
little “hells’’have been shut up the authorities
find themselves afraid to go any further, for fear
they might stumble across a prince. The places
raided last week would not be clubs, but “hells,”
where bookmakers were caught paying and
receiving money. A high-toned establishment
that books its bets is all right because the law
can’t arrest gamblers “on the nod.” Justice is
too fond of nodding itself.

A certain plunger just now out of business,
has always been ready to back his opinion.
When his father died and left the “ plunger ”
and a brother with some embarassed station-
property, the equity of redemption was sold for
£5OO each. The plunger said £5OO was no good to
him. So he went to Flemington and backed
horses. His luck was good, and he invested his
winnings in silver shares, which turned out
magnificently. Another instance of “ lightly come,
lightly go.”
SUICIDE OR NO SUICIDE.
The New York Life Insurance Company
Pays Its Policy.
It being evident that most cases of self-destruc-
tion involve causes not under the victim’s own
control, and that accident and murder may easily
give rise to a suspicion of voluntary death, the
New York Life Insurance Company, in order to
prevent wrongs to the unfertunate, makes its
policies absolute security. It exercises caution in
granting a policy, and never issues one unless its
officers consider the applicant a good risk. But
when it has once issued its policy and taken the
premium, it feels bound to insure. It does not
insert in its policies the usual clause making them
void in case of “self-destruction, voluntarily or
involuntarily, sane or insane,” and claims no re-
lease from its contract except where there is
evidence of a purpose to defraud. In short, so
far as a life policy may properly be such, the New
York Life’s policies are incontestable from the
moment the first premium is paid. The man with a
New York Life Insurance policy may feel sure that,
incase of his death, no delay, litigation or loss will
arise from the question of self-destruction—
whether it be accidental or otherwise. If he has
been honestly insured, and honestly conforms to
the simple requirements of the contract, the pay-
ment of his policy is sure on proof of his death.
Chief offices, department of Australasia, corner of
George and Bond streets, Sydney,

B EYOND COMPETITION.
The Waterbary Watches take front rank as
“the timekeepers of the day.” They are not
“English levers” made in Geneva, and puffed
into notoriety to enforce rales and ensure dis-
appointment. The factory, which is now turning
out over half a million of tlure watches per
annum—a production larger than that of any
other watch company in the world—is still unable
to keep pace with the demand, and has now
extended its operations into the very heart of the
cheap watch-making territory on the < outinent if
Europe, where it successfully competes with these
“English levers” on iqual terms. These are no
idle boasts, but stubborn facts, and we challenge
the refutation of these assertions. The Water-
burys (series hi, J, and L) are only nickel-plated
watches sold at a low figure, but th y get their
reputation from Ive purchasers and not the
owners of dead stock. They are far more reliable
timekeepers than half of the jewelled-all-over-
patent-halfcock-go-as-you-please-baik-action -hair-
trigger - compensated - lock - stitch - burglar - baits
made in Switzerland and Germany, and »o:d as
English levers. The Waterbary Watches have
nothing to fear from ridicule, no dread of ex-
posure. They are simply w hat they claim to be,
and defy competition.
These world-famed and time-tried watches may
be obtained from any respectable dealer or store-
keeper throughout the colonies. Prices : Series
E, the old favourite, 13s. 6d. ; Series J (gents’
size), and L (lad es’ size), the new short-winding,
stem-winding, and setting marvels, 225. Gd. each.
No watch sold as a Waterbury is genuine without
the word Waterbury and the interwoven letters
W. W. Co. printed in fine type upon its dial face.

THE PRAYER OF A RIGHTEOUS MAN.
(Reported by our Launceston Poet.)
Thau all-forgiving Lord of Love an ; d Light,,
Who when smitten didst not smite;
Thou who didst pardon one upon the accursed tree,
Eorgive to-night a viler thief than He ;■
A wretch corrupt in body and in heart j
A scavenger’s- cart j
A traitorous Judas; idiot, miscreant, fool,
Deformed, contemptible, a devil’s tool,
Ail eel whose slimy wngglings can’t be fixed,
An Ananias with a Human mixed,
A coward, sneak, a snake with venom stored,
Keviler of the Anointed of the Lord,
A LIAR I
Suffer him not to- be destroyed by fire !
Eorgive him ! Save him ! Bless !
Nevertheless —
Not my will, Lord, but Thy will be done t
If ThoU wilt cleanse this perjured Satan’s son
Make him so pure that lie may sit near Thee,
In Thy blest home, with Peter, Paul,- and Me !
If Thou wilt not,
Let Tommy Trot
His leprous soul with foulest evil crammed,
BE DAMNED !! 1

Dear Bulletin, — As book-canvassing has had very much
attention in the Press of late, I crave a small space to
give some interes:ing information of a historical character
concerning book-canvassers. While I do not for a moment
shield book-agent impostors, I do know that many book-
canvassers have rendered great public service in the dis-
semination of unimpeachable books. Thousands and
hundreds of thousands of good books have been annually
sold to people who, but for the canvasser, would never
have bought a book. The indefatigable agents have been
the pioneers of education into the remote st parts of the
Country, in many cases long before the Minister or school-
teacher visited the locality. I feel sure it will be a sur-
prise to your readers to learn that so many of the greatest
lights of the world have been book-agents. Bonaparte,
after the siege erf Toulon, when a young lieutenant, was
too honourable to duplicate his pay-account, so took the
agency for the “History of the Revolution.” It is
also said that Bonaparte travelled with a “ Life
o'f Cjosar,” and his prospectus, with the names of
0.5 Of his subscribers, was buried at Ids side. Bismarck,
tne greatest diplomatist of the age, was a book-canvasser.
Also Presidents Grant, Garfield, Abraham Lincoln, Jeffer-
son Davis, the Lord Mayor of London for 1835
(Joint O'Kelly), also Dan O'Connell, Josh. Billings,
Mark Twain, Bret Harte, Bob Ingersoll, Horace
Greeley, Wii.iam Hearts the lawyer and statesmen of
New York City, Madame De Stael and Mr. Jamieson.
Columbus also travelled on a work of marine exploration.
Washington, prior to the fated Braddock expedition,
sold over 200 copies of “The American Savage” in a
thinly-settled country in Virginia. Jay Gould, Ralph
Waldo Emerson, and Longfellow were canvassers, and
tile latter's success was remarkable. There is now in
possession of t'ne Massachusetts Historical Society a
prospectus which the poet used, and on one of the blank
leaves are the skeleton lines of the celebrated poem
“ Excelsior.” Dan. Webster was a canvasser, and paid
Ills second term of tuition at Dartmouth College by selling
books. James G. Biaine began his business career as a
book-canvasser in Washington, selling the “ Life of
Henry Clay.” Many others who emblazon the pages of
history attribute their success to the experience attained
while engaged in the vocation of canvassing. On reflec-
tion as to the character of books sold by canvassers, it
is surprising how few unworthy hooks have been distri-
buted, while the most useful works have been dis-
seminated, and most of them have been specialities not
procurable in any shop. I write hoping that merit will
meet its deserts in the punishment of fraudulent book-
canvassers, while the honest toiler may be encouraged by
knowing that so many illustrious men of the world have
in their turn been hook-agents.—Fair Play,
[Next!— Ed. B.]

offence has not yet been removed from the Queens-
land Statute-book. What will anyone bet that

This Manipur Disaster recals the worst in
Indian annals. It was the first Afghan Expedi-
tipn, 1842. Twelve thousand five hundred, in-
cluding camp - followers, were slain, mostly in
Kyber Pass, and only one individual escaped, Dr.
Brydone. Hence ensued the conquests of
Afghanistan, Scinde, Oude—and the Indian
Mutiny.

The wife of a workless London docker,
who appeared in a state of blue collapse from
hunger, and on seeing whom and hearing his
awful story the poor workmen of the jury actually
shed tears, recently bore triplets in a room which
contained nothing but a table and a chair ; every-
thing had been pawned for food—the windows
were dilapidated, the hearth was fireless, and the
elder children were crying of hunger. It was
elicited at the inquest that all the family had
been able to obtain for two days was “ a ha’porth
of tea, a ha’porth of sugar, and a ha’porth of
bread.” Yet the German pauper Battenberg and
countless other parasites of the worthless, scro-
fulous, miserly, gambling Royal Eamily annually
rob the State Treasury of thousands sweated out
of British flesh and coined from British blood.
Many people seeing the words “ Microbe
Killer” have been led to believe that it is a
disinfectant. It is not a disinfectant, but a
powerful medicine, for while it kills the germs of
disease, it does it in a different manner. It is
taken internally or applied externally as the case
may require. It attacks and kills the microbes in
the system which are the direct cause of disease.
After these germs are destroyed the medicine will
thoroughly cleanse the blood and tone up the
whole system—in short, it restores to a healthy
condition all the members of the body, Although
this medicine has only been for sale in Australia
a short time the constantly increasing sales bear
witness to the good it is accomplishing. People
who have been using the Microbe Killer for con-
sumption, kidney and liver troubles, indigestion,
and, in fact, for almost every disease, are loud in
their praises of its virtue. Being composed of
pure water charged with powerful germ destroyr

water, Bend for the book, ‘ ‘ History of Microbes
and the Microbe Killer,” posted free. The head

agencies have been established at 319 George-
street, Sydney ; 17 Hunter-street, Newcastle;

can be obtained,*

h The price I pai'l for Darkey ? Well, I’m blowed
If I can tell,
Becov I pot him dealing you see, boss—
I was always fond of dealin’ —as a boy I’d buy or
sell
With any other kid I came across.
“ You’re a scholar ; you can reckon up his figure
from the start : , ,
Ib< u lit a mare at Maffra, bay—bob-tail ; _
3he cost me seven sov'reigns and was branded With
a heart _ '
Near shoulder. Well, I rode her down to bale—
.< 4 n > there I met a feller on a big upstandin’ roan,
I swapped the mare for him. He didn't suit ;
His owner, from his back had twice that very day
been thrown,
I had to give him three-pounds-ten to boot.
u Throw me olf ? No, not exactly. Though he
tried it for a while,
But dropped it when he found it was no go,
Then I traded for a trotter that could do a level
In three minutes seven seconds— that ain’t slow!
“ But I didn’t swap him level; I put a saddle
down . . . ,
That cost three notes-receiving thirty bob
An a watch I raffled after-twenty members at a
cru vvn ;
An’ then I swapped the trotter for a cob
“ A natty little chestnut, but I bartered him off
quick
For a skewbald an’ a gun : I let her go
For a fiery little black horse that had got a nasty
trick
Of kickin’ every time he got a show.
“That gun was worth a fiver. I travelled round a
bit
An’ got another chestnut for the black,
An’ a bridle worth ten shillin’s; the chestnut was
real grit,
A well-bred, showy model of a hack.
“ But I traded her for Darkey, an’ a prize
retriever-pup ;
But trav’lin’ down to Toora it got lost.
I got eight notes with Darky, so, boss, reckon all
that up
An’ let me know exactly what he cost.”
J. A. MUIRHEAD.
Gov. Jersey is not a bad horseman, bub he
certainly looks anything but a good one. At the
review at Campbelltown he might easily have
been taken for a military Guy Fawkes. His
figure was as bent as Major I ongfield’s, and alto-
gether he was such a spectacle that the fluttering
plumes on his gorgeous hat seemed shaking with
laughter at the sight. Nevertheless he pulled
through all right, and was on hand to time at
luncheon.
Governor Hopetoun, attended by a mob of
fiery creatures in cocked hats, has laid the founda-
tion-stone of a Home for worn-out soldiers and
sailors. Not, of course, the common or field
kind of sailors, but the brave fellows who pass
their lives in polishing her Majesty’s guns and
fishing-up lost torpedoes from the bottom of
Hobson’s Bay. It has, we learn, long been a
reproach to Victoria— i.e., the Cabbage Garden—
that she allows her defenders to go on frightening
away the Russians without making any provision
for the time when they must hang their swords
and cutlasses against the kitchen-wall and tell
toothless anecdotes of the awful tilings they used
to do for the Hempire. While England has been
supporting Woolwich pensioners, and gloating
over pictures of those interesting old liars in the
illustrated papers, Victoria has altogether over-
looked the value of decayed heroes as advertise-
ments for the sham-battle business. But now she
is awakened to the necessity of having a few
veterans, so that the Governor and his leading
braves can make “ feeling references ” to the
United Service Home, whose inmates have served
their country so faithfully and well.” (Loud
cheers.) What troubles the authorities at present
is the absurd physical completeness of candi-
dates for the Home. They haven’t lost any
arms or legs, and not one of them has
had the middle of his back carried away by a
cannon-ball. As picturesque reminders of the
horrors of war, they can’t compare with the em-
ployes of an serated-water factory, most of those
latter being minus a few fingers or an eye at the
very least. Consequently, there is some talk of
making amputa ion of a limb indispensable for
veterans wishing to enter the Home, and the man
who has most limbs sawn off will get first choice
of rooms. The pubbc expect wooden-legs and
cork-arms of worn-out warriors, and unless the
old- ’uns can point to a lot of honourable scars and
bullet-holes, the public begin to wonder —very
properly, too —whether there is any sense in keep-
ing such people out of work all their lifet me.
This is a view of the question which the public
must not be allowed to take. Therefore, we may
expect to find that Dr. Fitzgerald, or somebody,
will be requested to visit the Vic. naval and
military forces from time to time for the purpose
of chopping a lump off any long-service loafer who
wishes to retire into the Home on his laurels.

Queen Victoria, had the “ Gondoliers’’ acted before her
lately at Windsor Castle — only the second occasion of her
seeing a play since Albert died, the other being the
comedy of “The Colonel,” by Bruce’s Co., at Abergeldie
Castle. The “Gondolier” people had a high old time,
being driven home to London in twelve omnibuses—out
of the taxes,
Here’s about Indian Viceroy Lytton—who now, while
British Ambassador at Paris, has just entertained Melba
—and the circus-rider, lair little Victoria Cooke, who
came over here with Wilson’s Circus. She was the wife of
Professor Schiemann, who succeeded Omar Kingsley
(Ella Zoyara) as equestrian director. The occurrence was
after tnat magnificent bogus Punch and Judy, the Im-
perial Durbar, at Delhi, where George Titheradge recited
the “Ode,” and medals were given out to all the nigger
princes. Lytton had some on hand and was bothered,
till the glorious idea struck him of stepping into the
circus-ring and presenting Victoria - not Her Majesty, but
Victoria Cooke—with one of the medals, as Empress of
the Arena ! Yates raked it up and ripped it open. What
a stinging satire ! You see, even Viceroy Lytton had his
tongue in his cheek at the Durbar.
“Carados,” of the London Refkreb, says: “Most
stranded ballet-girls that I have met are clergymen’s
daughters, and can do something else for a living.”
Lily Langtry’s dollars are going the way of those of
most manageresses. Her new play, “Lady Barter,” is
another black frost.
A Miss Kentish Moore, who was recently “ commanded’
to sing at Osborne, seems to have quite “ knocked ” our
Gracious Queen. The old lady rewarded her with a
diamond bracelet and a chaste salute on the cheek.
The late Barnum made more celebrity by his purchase
of Jumbo than by any of his thousands of better financial
speculations. And if that perverse beast (who after all
was only a trifle bigger than ordinary elephants) hadn’t
pitted himself against a railway train, there was a fortune
in him for the old showman. Jumbo possessed no feature
of interest, save the advertised fact that he was extra
large, a circumstance for which the world didn’t care a
straw until Barnum worked it up into a great Anglo-
American question. But the newspaper drivel, the stories
of how the British nobility sent barrels of oysters for the
elephant, in order that he might be “kept up” in the
hour of his departure for the Far West, all this bunkum
had sunk deep into the American soil. Englishmen
travelling through the States found themselves special
objects of interest. The girls would cluster round young
Adolphus and say—“l guess you’ve seen Jumbo—what’s
he like?” Whereupon the globe-trotter would expand his
chest and reply, “ Haw, a dem’d fine elephant, bai jove 1”
The great Melbourne alligator was found to have the
photo of a lady hidden away, together with 64 stones, a
yard of fencing wire, and other dainty morsels, in close
proximity to his “sweetbread.”
Lord Londesborough, who long figured as the king of
“patrons” of stage beauty, has become respectable, and
goes about with his daughters. It was Londesborough
who found the money for Boucicault’s production of
“ Babil and Bijou,” and the great Dion, being safely in-
stalled as lessee of the theatre (Covent Garden) soon
ordered his lordship to clear out from oehind the scenes.
Emily Fowler, Florence Gerard, and a whole lot of beauti-
ful girls were “ brought out ” by this long, lean, faded-
looking earl. Most of them developed into real actresses,
too.
Wybert Reeve now runs Adelaide Theatre Royal him-
self. His term commenced with the Cartwright-Nethersole
season. Wybert maintains his exuberantly youthful
style.
A Galway gossoon lately worked the slot trick to per-
fection. He used a hole-y penny with string attached,
put that penny in the slot, and drew his toffee, but pulled
the coin out again, and carefully repeated the perform-
ance until he had emptied the machine and was able to
start as a toffee-hawker. He was arrested for theft, but a
Galway Justice summed the matter up:—“Always be
dutiful and do exactly as you’re told, and no more and
no less. The inscription on the machine said : * Drop a
penny in the slot.’ Yezdidso faithfully and repatedly.
The inscription on the machine said nothing about lavin’
the penny in the slot when it was wanst dropped in. You
conformed with the rules and ye’ve won your prizes. Go
and prosper agin.”
Had Charles Coghlan, Mrs. Langtry’s leading man,
chaffed the counsel for his creditors, “played to the
house,” and treated his shameful indebtedness as lightly
in an Australian bankruptcy court as he did in a British
one, he’d have been promptly committed for contempt.
Possibly he’d have met similar severity in London had he
been a’ “thirty bob” instead of a fifty pounds weekly
man. Coghlan never kept any books or accounts; em-
ployed a Belgian chef, and occasionally, while mail-coach
driving for instance, nineteen other servants ; rented a
house at £2OO a year, also chambers for himself at a cost
of £4 w'eekly, and rooms at Richmond for his wife at £2 ;
occasionally owned ten horses and hired others; owed
everyone money, from tailor, butcher, baker, grocer and
governess down to washerwoman, and had apparently no
desire to pay anyone.

steamer Pacific, by Dick Younge and Fanny Cathoart.
Fanny broke her engagement, on marrying Bob Heir,
who was picked up in Australia, and taught by her
to act; but the grievance was that the Napoleon of the
Drama wanted to marry Fanny.
The Opera House pit, Melbourne, was edified the other
night by a bucolic mother, who fetched a bevy to see the
spectacle. Fair enough it was till she saw her first ballet,
when she “ shoved ” all her clutch away from the debasing
spectable.

The four-year-old daughter of a well-known Sydney
opera singer woke her mother in the middle of the night
a few weeks ago with the question: “If God was Gentle
Jesus’ father, who was His grandpapa?” '
A London paper, having been idiotic enough to state
that Haddon Chambers, in accepting a commission to
write two plays for Daniel Frohman was “not doing him-
self justice in thus undertaking to write down to the
level of American audiences,” Frohman points out that
“The Idler” and “Maister of Woodbarrow”—two
London successes—were first produced in New York, and
the playwright himself asserts that he has “found
American audiences to be at once the most alert, appre-
ciative, critical and sympathetic of any to whom my plays
have hitherto been presented.”
Ushers who undertake to give bouquets to artistes ought
really to keep out of sight of the songstress, who (though
she, of course, knows all about it from the first) must be
more or less embarrassed when she sees the top fronds of
the lurking vegetables peeping round the door. It dis-
counts that charming look of surprise, too, and makes
people smile cynically.
Theatrical critics have a habit of perching together in
the dress-circle. They form a sort of conversational
stew-pan, and you can see the colouring through all the
reports. On the contrary, they ought to sit as separate
as biled owls, and glower at each other like Pott and
Slurk.

In a serious drama a prisoner was supposed to read
aloud a very long letter brought to him by the gaoler.
To save memorising, it was written out and one night
the gaoler, a practical joker, handed the prisoner a
blank sheet of paper. For a moment the prisoner was
nonplussed, but recovering himself and handing back the
paper, with a bow, said, “Gaoler ! I am obliged to make an
avowal to you. Brought up by parents of low estate, 1
do not know how to read 1 I beg that you will have the
goodness to acquaint me witn the contents of this letter.”
The snarer was snared, but his wit saved him, too.
After nervously fumbling the letter the gaoler Said,
“ Willingly, but I must go and look for my spectacles.”
