Does anyone rem mi her Jemmy Stride, the celebrated
“ box-keeper ” at Drury Lane, in the days of Chatterton ?
Because he recently died at the age of 74. Jemmy was
celebrated for his hat and his wholesale distribution of
“ orders,” in which branch of usefulness the old character
was mainly employed. Stride’s tall silk hat glistened like
a steel helmet in his halcyon days. It was never spotted
by rain nor specked with dust, and anybody else’s hat,
being worn for the first time, seemed dull and gloomy in
the presence of this incomparable tile. The story went
that a private detective 'received instructions to discover
the reason of Stride’s hat, but could trace the mystery no
further than a certain shop, where every morning one hat
would be left in exchange for another of the exact same
shape and glossiness. Two or more hats were evidently
turn and turn about, but the special “ fake-
ment ” practised upon them remained a secret between
Jemmy and the shopkeeper who used him as an adver-
tisement. _ With his hat for a card of introduction
Jemmy ingratiated himself with the wealthiest
of the professional racing world and made a
practice of posting “orders” to people who
would respond to the summons in evening-dress and a
carriage. “Large and fashionable” audiences were
often brought together by Jemmy’s discreetly-placed
“ paper,” and it was a treat to behold him bowing,
smiling, and raising the renowned hat in honour of a
crowd of people who were going in “on the never.”
Once a year this master of the paper-hanging art enjoyed
a benefit, and he was original even in that. Instead of
waiting for his acquaintances to take tickets, Jemmy
posted tickets to them, just as many as he thought they
would pay for, and in fair proportion to the number of
orders they had respectfully received during the twelve
months. But it must be many a long year since
“ Stride’s Benefit ” breathed its last, and Stride’s hat
began to lose its lustre. There are no great, Napoleonic
“ box-keepers” nowadays.
They call the Queensland soldiers “ the
Offensive Force ” now.
Weighted down as most newspapers are
by the necessity of currying favour with adver-
tisers, of ignoring scandals against moneyed
persons and influential syndicates, and of cutting
their political coats according to the state of their
bank overdrafts, the sentiments of the majority of
the Australian press concerning the shearers’
strike may be condensed into—“ Bloodshed ? A
little bloodshed would do no harm, provided that
the right sort of blood is shed, provided that the
gore spilt is the common plebeian gore of low
unionists. But bloodshed of squatters? Or of
scabs? Murder —massacre—outrage—red revolu-
tion—demonry !” What is all this screaming
worth ?
Speakingat the recent big A. N, A. pow-wow,
at Ararat, ex-boss-native Purves, Q,C., replying to
the toast, “Australian Federation,” said among
other things that they could not always remain
an integral part of Great Britain ; and that “ one
of the main principles of Federation was a
universal fiscal policy—intercolonial Freetrade
and Protection against the outside world. Their
principal supplies were drawn from Great Britain,
and if they shut their doors against Great Britain
that meant fiscal separation. Let them not be
frightened at the word ? It meant separation.
They were only valued by Great Britain as con-
sumers ; and did they wish to be protected by
people whose goods they would not buy ? ” Which
shows a considerable change of front on the part
of the vacillating Australian Natives, whose new
president, Mr. G. Wise, talks like a straight-out
Republican.
Lars Peter Hansen is ordered to die for
the murder of his mate Duncker. He says he
killed Bucket in self-defence. The Judge re-
marked that any honest man who in self-defence
had killed another, would have given himself up
to the police instead of burning the body and
decamping. That argument is a specious one,
for there is after all no policy like that of
Bismarckian candour. On the other hand, how-
ever, there are many arguments, based on what
one hears of the style in which the law and the
police operate, that would tend to uncertainty of
action on the part of the weak man who found
him elf in so awful a position. The circumstances
under which Hansen killed Duncker in the lojiely
bush will never be known for certain. Meanwhile
the law has convicted, and is going to hang, Hansen
on a hypothesis which may be correct, but is just
as likely to be incorrect. You can logically
deduce nothing as to a man’s criminality from the
fact that he tells you lies when he finds himself
threatened with an ignominious death.
The N.S.W. Premier at St. Leonards :
I have been surprised to understand how some gentle-
men who ought to be independent had such a craving
desire to become members of the nominee Legislative
Council. I was once pestered by a gentleman to recom-
mend him for the Council. I turned the subject, but he
worked back to the question of his fitness for the Council.
He saw me again and again, and finally on one occasion
said that if a cheque for £lO,OOO were lying on the table
and if the written offer of a seat were also”lying there he
would take the seat and he did hot care who took the
cheque. That was rather stiff, and I then asked him not
to see me again. I cite this anecdote because it is within
the knowledge of several very upright men, and to show
to what lengths.men will go to get into the Legislative
[ Council for life and for enjoying the paltry prefix of the
honor.

as due by him to men whom he appointed to the
Council aforesaid.

Tales of the Old Regime,
No. V.— The Immersion of Captain Bankes.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
We were going to tell you to-day the story
of the loss of the George 111., but, on second
thoughts, instead, will narrate a story which in
itself is a not uncharacteristic expression of the
ways of the Old Regime, though, in point of grim-
ness, compared with the manner of the going-
down of the George HI., it is a glimpse of sunshine
in Cimmerian darkness. This incident happened
in the year of Grace 1823, while it was not till the
year of the System and the Regime 47—that is to
say, 1835 —Neptune, and , a subordinate of the
Regime between them by the agency of a sunken
rock and soldiers’ bullets removed 120 names from
the penal roll. What, their, has the one to do
with the other ? Nothing very much beyond this:
the man, an ex-Wesley an missionary, who tells
you, through our pen, the story of Captain
Bankes’ drowning, will also tell you of the drown-
ing and the shooting of the convicts whom the
men of the Regime killed in D’Entrecastreaux
Channel.
# # #
Captaki Bankes, indefatigable and esteemed
officer of the System, after a call of duty had
taken Lieutenant Darrell from the Settlement,*
felt somewhat lonely in his apartments on Sarah
Island. His quarters had once been graced by
the presence of ladies, but those representatives
of the fair sex he had been reluctantly compelled
to remit to Hobart Town in order that they might
be formally released on the expiration of the sen-
tences for which they had been transported. The
ladies in question had been two in number, and
notwithstanding the inducement held out to them
by Captain Bankes to remain, they were drawn
by the fascinations of the Capital to its pave.
They had every comfort at Macquarie Harbour
except that sweet solace of the feminine soul—
shopping. There were no shops at Macquarie
Harbour, save the butcher’s shop you must
not’ coitfound this with Gallows-place—though
there was a store. As a recreation, Bankes had
allowed them the run of the Store, and had even
gone so far as to procure for them from Hobart
Town sundry fal-di-rals, dress materials, and other
women’s gear, which the Captain, carrying his
gallantry still further, paid for through the
System by docking the men's rations or
clothing allowance. But Robertson’s shop in
Maoquarie-street they call it Ingle Hall
now, and Robertson’s cattle-brand has carried
the fame of the Oolag swards over the Aus-
tralias and Lewis I ', in Murray-street, em-
poriums, as they were, of the latest London and
Paris fashions —were irresistible, and, conse-
quently, the two beauties insisted upon being
returned to the Capital. Captain Bankes and
Lieutenant Darrell had seen them off by the
Cyprus, and the former had flicked his handker-
chief to them as the schooner passed the point of
Phillip Island.
“ Deuce take it!” said Bankes to Darrell, as
the women returned the salute, “I won’t know
what to do with myself, now that the girls are
gone and you are going after pine to the
Gordon.”
Darrell grinned. “Get some more,” he
suggested.
Bankes, slapping his hand on his knee, cursed
his own stupidity in not writing)' by the outgoing
schooner, to the Principal Superintendent of Con-
victs for a fresh contingent of Cyprians.
Resourceful Darrell was again ready with a
suggestion. “ Signal her back,” he said.
No sooner said than doneto the detriment of
Skipper Jobson’s temper, the vessel was recalled,
and told to wait for a fresh despatch. He was
going out on a fair wind, and with the tide in his
favour, but the couple of hours he lost in tacking
to secure Captain Bankes’ love-missive spoilt his
chances of a fair run. Which, of course, was
nothing to Captain Bankes.
# # #
Let us stand by the shoulders of Mr, J.
Fubster as he opens the letter :
Macquarie Harbour,
9th June, 1823.
Dear Fubster,—
In accordance with your official memo., as I have
officially written you, the women who have been down
here are returned for discharge by this boat. Like
a good fellow, arrange for two or three more to return
by this Cyprus. Thanks to your demand for more pine,
I have to send Darrell 30 miles away for timber, and,
therefore, I am entirely thrown on my own resources for
society. The Doctor is generally drunk—of course, Ido
not state this as an official complaint, for he might
retaliate by saying the same thing of me- and except for
some of the “ special”! convicts, there isn’t a living soul
I can enjoy a word of rational conversation with, and
then to give those fellows the run of Officers’ Quarters is
just to provoke discontent and rebellion among the
men whom (don’t laugh !-) I don’t honor with the privilege
of associating with me. Why, only the other night,
Darrell and I sent for Lavington and Arnold—you
remember the two doctors who were sent out for their
Dublin insurance swindle ?-to take hands at whist.
Lavington, like a fool, couldn’t keep the invitation to
himself, but must brag of it, so another fellow (a solicitor,
or barrister, or writer, or something) swore he shouldn’t
go. Lavington wanted to know how he was going to be
kept from going.
“I’ll show you,” said the other rascal, as he knocked
him down with the bed-rail.
Lavington is still in hospital, and I had to give the other
fellow fifty!, as a warning not to spoil ray card-parties in
future, for if there is a thing I do hate, it is playing with
or against dummy. But here lam rambling on and have
got away from the main point—which is, don’t let'ine be
lonely for long. You may have some little difficulty with
the superior powers, but you can get over that.
With kindest regards,
Ever yours faithfully,
(Sd.) Frederick B. Bankes.
J. Fubster, Esq.,
Prim Sup. of Convicts, H.T.,
For Ms private hand.
* * *
That esprit de-corps which is supposed to
exist among rogues was certainly not lacking in
the gentlemen of the Old Regime. And though
when Bankes’ despatch reached the city, Gover-
nor Sorell was in one of those cantankerous moods
which, really due to bile, be was accustomed to
attribute to virtue, and though on that account
he could not be depended upon to be complaisant
in the matter of the Commandant’s request, Mr.
Jaraes Fubster had to rely upon his own machinery
for transferring fdur female transports from the
Hobart Town factory to the Officers’ Quarters on
Sarah Island. To tell you the truth, we cannot
say that Mr. Fubster had occasion to put himself
to very much trouble to secure that end. There
were a few formalities to be gone through which
were rendered necessary by the circumstance that
the System had in such matters to display an out-
ward deference to decency, for the Harbour was,
officially speaking, a “male settlement,” pure and
simple. These, however, were summarily gone
through with the result that Jane Applegarth
(23), Minnie Reado (18), Fanny Cockburn (21),
and Julia Perry (26), were assigned to Mr. John
Smith, settler, District of Lake St. Clair, by
order of the Prim Sup. of Convicts. That was
formality the first, done to satisfy the conscience
of the Matron _of the Factory. Formality the
second was an intimation to the keepers of the
keys of the military chest, that he must requisi-
tion the Colonial Secretary for an order to send
per Cyprus “the wives of Privates Applegarth,
Reade, Cockburn, and Perry, men of the garrison
at Macquarie Harbour, to their husbands’)(quar-
ters.” You will have no difficulty in understand-
that Mr. John Smith and the quartette of hus-
bands were alike non-existent. They wefe official
fictions in fact. Mr. John Smith used often to
play the part of John Doe and Richard Roe in
the little games of the Regime.
It was in this way that Mr. Fubster obliged his
friend Bankes, and thus the Misses Applegarth,
Reade, Cockburn, and Perry who, new arrivals
from that dear land which had given thfem birth,
and then sought the first opportunity of giving
them death, and who had kissed their co-mates
and sisters in exile as they left the factory at the
Brickfields for the district of Lake St. Clair, were
somewhat surprised to find themselves ushered to
the Old Wharf, thence into a boat rowed by
prisoners, and taken on board the Government
schooner, whose “Blue Peter” was up for the
Hell of the Harbour.
In the fullest innocence of their true destina-
tion, moral and physical, these girls went on
board. They were received with nominal
courtesy. Fubster, in his solicitude for Captain
Bankes’ interests, had bestowed upon the
schooner’s skipper, a kindly and genial and
therefore unofficial hint, that a course of good
manners and chivalrous conduct was very desir-
able, and, consequently, from the skipper to the
mate, and from the sergeant of the guard to the
steward, the authorities of the schooner—who
were, let us say, pretty big authorities in their
way, for they had the power (and not infre-
quently exercised it) of dropping an ounce of lead
into a rebellious convict’s skull —acted on the hint,
and treated the young ladies with every form of
respect during the three weeks that the passage
lasted. The girls were thus induced to congratu-
late themselves upon the change in their circum-
stances. Seldom have congratulations proved
more premature.
* * *
When making Cape Sorell, at the entrance
to Macquarie Harbour, the girls ventured upon
deck, which they had been only occasionally
enabled to visit during the voyage owing to the
boisterousness of the weather. The wind had
lulled, and as the vessel tacked to and fro,
waiting the arrival of the pilot-boat, the girls,
holding on to a rope which had been run
across the after-deck for their convenience,
speculated as to their future. They had not
been long “out,” as has been said, but it was,
nevertheless, surprising that some knowledge
of the infamies of the Harbour Settlement had not
reached their ears. So, however, it was. For
aught they knew, the entrance to Macquarie
Harbour might be also the portal to that terres-
trial paradise, a good man’s home, where each
would be legally installed as wife and mistress.
They knew, certainly, that it was not thg way to
John Smith’s homestead, but they were unaware
of the thousand-and-one ways the System had of
disposing of the bodies and souls of the trans-
ports. And the politeness and kindness which
had marked the marine officer’s treatment of
them was such as to dispossess them of any par-
ticularly alarming ideas of the future that might
have awaited them. The Skipper, in response to
their enquiries as to what they would do at the
Harbour, had simply bade them “ wait and see !”
“You must know, my dears,” he said, pater-
nally, “ we never lets on as to what the big-bugs
do. You see they don’t like it, but don’t you be
fearful o’ nothink, pretties, for Cap’n Bankes is a
real gentleman.”
“Yes, he is a real orficer and gen’l’rnan is
Bankes !” corroborated Bowser, the mate. (The
conversation was at the semi-family gathering at
the cabin dinner-table.)
And the two men laughed. And the four girls
smiled —re-assured.
When the pilot-boat at last came up, the girls
saw seated in the stern of the boat a military
officer wearing an undress uniform, who, as soon
as he sprang up the schooner’s side, was respect-
fully saluted by Bowser, the skipper being down
in his cabin getting ready his manifest and other
papers.
“This ’ere’s a special honour, Captain,” said
Bowser —“yer cornin’ down with the boat. We
did not expect to ’ave the pleasure o’ seein’ you,
sir, till we got to the Island.”
“H’m. But I am rather anxious to see what
passengers you have.”
There were twenty-three prisoners down in the
hold, but the Captain did not mean them; those
were labelled cargo.
“ Four private sodgers’ wives, sir!” said Bowser.
Bowser, of course, knew better, but, then, he was
speaking by the book, or rather by the ship’s
papers. Bankes was not to know that he and his
skipper saw through the little game, as they did
see through it. Old birds are not to be caught
with chaff. For the moment Bankes, not sus-
pecting that Mr. Fubster had complied with his
request, by a swindle upon the military treasury,
was annoyed and aggrieved.
“ Four privates’ wives !” he repeated, in tones of
exceeding disgust. “You don’t mean to say,
Bowser, that’s all ?” Mr.' Bowser turned, and,
with a salute and a bow that would not have dis-
credited a master of ceremonies in the ball-room at
Government House, Hobart, or at a dance in the
Whitechapel Out, pointed out to Bankes the four
demoiselles who stood clinging to the rope, and
swinging gracefully with the undulation of the
vessel.
“Them’s the ladies,” says Bowser. “They’re
on the papers as sodgers’ wives, but I’m a-thinkin’,
sor, as that must be a clerical herror !”
* # *
Captain Bankes followed the direction of
Mate Bowser’s gesture, and took his cap off as he
approached the girls.
Quite the officer and the gentleman was Captain
Bankes ! So the girls thought.
# * #
“H’m,” said the Captain, bowing. “Ah
—h’m, very pleased to see you, ladies. Ah—eh—
let me welcome you —to —er—Macquarie Harbour.
Do hope you—er —haven’t had too rough a pas-
sage ?”
All the girls tittered, two curtsied, one found
her tongue and said—

Forgetting for the moment the presence of

Of course he found it, and as we have already
exposed his own letter to your notice, it is only
right we should now permit you to glance over his
shoulder at Fubster’s reply§ :—■
lI.M. Penal Department, V.D.L.,
Principal Superintendent’s Office,
Hobart Town, 21 | 7 | 23.
My dear Captain,—
Yours of the 9th June duly to hand, with the last
batch of despatches, and as you will see by this boat, I
have done my best for you. You asked for one, two or
three of the sex, but, as a touching proof of my friend-
ship, I have sent you four. They are all more or less
long-termers, so whoever you select for the post of house-
keeper, you will not be soon discomposed by having to
lose her. I send the four to give you every opportunity
of making a permanent selection, but, if on the other
hand, you want variety, that condition also will be met.
Of course, old man, Sorell doesn’t know of the business.
He is at the present time unwell, and, therefore, more
than usually inclined to put his foot down upon our
fellows, and I thought it best not to even hint to him
anything of your request, as, for one thhig, he is tired of
the present Madame at Gov. House, and were he to sus-
pect that there were four or five fine-looking ladies among
the factory mob, Pm afraid Madame would get her
conge, which wouldn't suit mo just now. As I could
not tell His Most Noble, I have had to work the usual
manoeuvre upon the matron, of the Factory, who has
just roused to a sense of her' sinfulness through
the agency of a Methodist humbug, named Turner.
Shouldn’t be at all surprised to find the old sinner
—I mean Turner, not the matron - has won over
theold woman to get the girls himself. You will phase
therefore note I have put you down as Mr. Smith, District
of Lake St. Clair—a gentleman I’m afraid who is rather
over-running his allowance of women convicts, as these
four make 35 within a few months. (You military gents
are too altogether luxurious.) I am sending them to you
as private soldiers’ wives. You will take care as (Military
officer in command to keep your records straight, for
your Major is talking of a trip to the Harbour to inspect,
and the paymaster for that reason will want a voucher as
to the girls’ passage. You can arrange so that the old
man shall not ask about the soldiers’ wives. Now as to
the girls, Cockburn is my fancy, she can piny, sing, and
dance a little. The accomplishments of the others are
very much a matter of opinion, but I should think they
all suit yon As soon as you are tired of
’nil, let me know, and if you can’t marry them down
there, send’em back and I will give “John Smith’’credit,
for their return. Now, as I have obliged you in this
little matter,“you must help me. Old Strode and I (not
the young one) have indented from home some capital
port, blit the quantity is larger than we ordered. Can
you do witli three dozen ? If so, don’t fail to let me know
what I shall charge them as. Don’.; let it be handcuffs
again this time, old fellow, or shirts—the Commissariat
can’t put through any more of either till (hey have
squared off the billiard-table account. Besides, one or
two expirees who were lodging with you say that you
didn’t give any fresh shirts for nine months. The Commy
people say you can have your choice of rice, leg-irons,
canvas trousers, serge ditto, and straw hats. The port is
86s. a n—that is what it cost us, and we will let you
mTwV'wnivf man'll not sa .Y Mis Most Gracious
Majesty won t touch the duty upon it. Old Knobvvood,
here at this moment-sends his pastoral blessing—hopes
you read prayers as per Regulation. Told him no doubt
of it. I must now close, wishing you joy and happiness
with the new arrivals.
Yours truly,
J. Fubster.
P.S.—Judge Field goes home shortly, and it is wins-
pered we are at last to have a charter of our own. If so,
it will not be long before we have a separate Government,
and then— i mention this so that you may be pre-
pared for developments.— J. F.

and then, turning to Jobson ; “ Now, Jobson,

“ Honour bright, Cap’n ! I know yOu wouldn’t
like addled eggs !” ’
. * *
As the schooner, in charge of the pilot—
an expatriated Holyhead “ boy ”—ran up to the
Island, the girls had time to further discuss their
prospects, to watch Bankes as he opened, first one
official scrawl, and then another, and to observe
tho sombre outlines with which Nature closed in
the basin. Miles inland from the head of the
Harbour, the “Frenchman’s ” held up his “Cap”
of lofty, snow-decked granite, as though to salute
the weird genii of the neighbourhood. From the
Cap trailed streamers of greyish mist into the
scrubby gullies, and over the bald ridges of the
lesser heights. On the southern side of the inlet
the dark-green forests of myrtle were here and
there relieved by a flash of a softer tint, as an
occasional tall pine reared its pyramidal and
elegant form to which clung, pendulously, its rows
of tooth-edged branehlets. The more vivid gleam
indicated how the erratic laws of vegetable growth
had dropped into the midst of valueless scrub the
precious wood which could only be redeemed from
its worthless contiguity by the sacrifice of a
human existence; for it was a tradition amongst
the timber gang of tho Harbour that a tree which
had been so isolated by the time it had been
brought to market had cost some poor sinful Son
of the System his life. On the other side
tho aspect was even mote sombre. Where now
stands tlie township of Stratum, with its
hustling, bustling crowd of miners and miners’
parasites, there stood then, dense, impenetrable
blocks of blackwood, of ti-tree, and myrtle.
From the north and south there came shadow
and sombreness, but, right east, in the direct path
to the woody curve whence, so it seemed to the
girls’ eyes in the misty perspective, there rose the
half-veiled mass of the mountains, there lay Sarah
Island with its verdant base, and its superstrnc-
ture of brick and stone buildings, and high-paled
stockade. And upon that nest of misery there
rested a beam of sunlight which, had it broadened
its glory over the whole aspect of the Harbour
would, perchance, have cheered the most drooping
heart which was fretting away its tenderness
within the precincts of the Settlement. Cockburn,
pretty Fanny Cockburn, who had nourished her
stupid, little, fane ful brain, upon the novels
which taught tho lower and middle classes of
England how My Lord and My Lady loved and
lived in marble halls with wondrous tapestry on
their walls that had been fashioned by the fingers
of Queens, and so forth. This little girl, slim,
\va« Nr'i’vl.w. 1 Hetuen knows what
was lii i ciimc. probably, m being tho sport of
some great man s fancy and wickedness (Botany
Bay freed many a great man from an encum
brance), thought this athwart glance of sunlight
was ominous of good.
“See,” she said to Applegarth—“See, Janie,
that is the place, I ’spose ! Don’t it look bright
and gay ? Happy is the bride, they say, that the
sun shines on, and perhaps, you know, we may be
brides there yet.”
Poor wretch !—“ Brides !!”
# * *
Passing Kelly’s Basin ; Bankes once more
approached the girls. He felt he had made an
impression on one of them, at least, and his
masculine vanity being propitiated—he liked to
be loved for his own sake, did Bankes !—he felt it
was high time to assert his official character.
“Well, girls”—he spoke imperiously—“what’s
your names ? Yours !”
“Jane Applegarth, sir.” Jane answered re-
spectfully enough, but Bankes voted her truculent.
He meant virtuous —he knew something of the
sex.
“ You must answer more civilly, my girl, if you
don’t want your head shaved.”
The tears rose to Applegarth’s eyes, but she
forced them down resolutely, and answered noth-
ing.
“ Yours ? ”
“Fanny Cockburn, sir— C-o-c-k-b-u-r-n, sir.”
Fanny was proud of the orthography of her name
(she was not a common Coburn) and replied
minoingly, her eyelashes the while half-veiling
oh ! such a killing look. Fanny would have flirted
with the hangman on “ her day.” ||
The Commandant twitched his moustache.
That was Cockburn then —Mr. Fubster’s “ fancy.”
Well, seeing as he did eye to eye with the Prim
Sub. of Convicts in many things, he did not
hesitate in permitting his judgment to coincide
with that esteemed friend’s in the matter of
Cockburn’s charms.
“ Er—please stand aside, my de—, my girl.”
“Yours?” to the third girl. Minnie Reade
answered.
“Then, you are Perry ?” to the fourth.
“Yes, sir, please—Julia Perry.” This with a
curtsey ns graceful as the shifting deck wculd
allow.
“ And what can you do, Perry !”
“ Knit, sir, and sew, and cook—”
“Yes, yes; I know. All women can do these
things, I s’pose !” Bankes answered, impatiently.
“ But what else can you do ? I make men do that
sort of thing here !”
Perry (infanticide, “lifer”) a quiet damsel,
with features not so much pretty as pleasing, was
about to respond, when the big-boned and large-
hearted Jane intervened.
“ She can’t do nothin’ else, sir !”
“Who asked you to speak, madam? Under-
derstand you’re here to be spoken to, and not to
speak. ”
“ I know, sir!” Again the tears rushed for-
ward to blind the courageous girl, and again they
were sent back by her resolution that she would
do her duty. She spoke submissively but with
firmness.
“You do know ! Then why do you not let
Perry answer for herself? What can you do,
Perry ? Can you sing or play the harp ?”
“ Oh, sir, No! But I would make a soldier a
good wife, sir, I would ! I wouldn’t go wrong
again, sir.”

copied verbatim hero with the exception of certain
nameless terms and unreporlable phrases. The present

While making this statement he does not, however, wish
it to be Understood tiiat the letter itself is an invention.
It is not so; it is simply an adaptation of extant cor-
respondence written by the Commandant of another
“Male” Station.

the date as peculiarly theirs.

“Wife!” Over the beat of the surge, and the
rustle of the cape-pigeon’s wings as in a dense
flock they followed the schooner to her anchorage,
resounded the derisive laughter of Captain Bankes,
Commandant at Macquarie Harbour.
“Wife! That is rich, ray sweet lass! We
don’t marry, except over the broomstick, here.”
* * *
The waves caught and sent back his
laughter, and the woods returned the echo of his
words—but all the women, even the Cockburn,
looked as though they heard not, for they were
stunned at the brutal avowal. Applegarth, who
had suspected from Bankes’ opening words
something, felt the shock most acutely. Neverthe-
less, she was the first to speak.
“ Then—what—are we - to be, sir ?”
There are some terms which were appropriately
included in the vocabulary of the high-toned
parties of the Old Regime, probably in order that
they should be at no loss to understand the dialect
of Tophet on their arrival there, but which, never-
theless, are yet not to be mentioned in decent
society.
Captain Bankes’ reply consisted of one of these
words.
“Never ! ” said Applegarth. Cockburn put her
hands before her face—prettily shocked; the
others burst into tears. Jobson laughed in concert
with the fine young British gentleman of the new
colonial time.
* * #
“Never!” Jane had said.
(( Captain Bankes forgot his manners, and swore.
Damn you, you jade. You —— ! ” The
emphasis he placed upon that unreputable ropeti-
®l(® •. “Well see ! Jobson, put her in irons !”
(( Sir !—Captain Bankes ! ”
As I order, sir, and no nonsense ! ” He
dropped the despatch-bag which he had held in
ms hands up to that moment, and from a pocket of
his uniform-coat, drew out a pair of darbies and
advanced to where the dauntless girl was asserting
her own and her co-transports’ Womanliness—
But out your hands!” he commanded. The
girl answered him by putting one behind her ; the
other still clung to the rope.
Bhe was on one side—the tiller side-of the
rope, and he was on the other. He stretched out
ms hand to grasp hers, when the Skipper- who
ia , in his heart, resolved to outer into relations
V l6 fjrls, Applegarth for choice—
caned to the helmsman to “Luff!” The com-
and was, doubtless, necessitated by the exi-
gencies of .seamanship, but it had the effect of
upsetting Mr., Bankes’ foothold and his dignity,
f in ,® , e luffing of the craft, the Commandant

‘ J f '“ a heap of incensed officialdom at Apple-
garth’s feet.

then—and then—

“ Do, coward ! —Cnr ! But if you come near me

“Iron her, Jobson, iron her !” ordered the mad-

“ Yes, iron me—iron a woman ! Why don’t you

me ! ”
“By I will, too, you she-devil ! Bowser,
the bracelets, man, quick—where are they ? ”
Bowser was hard put to it to restrain a grin.
Still he respectfully answered : “ They went over-
board with y’r Honor, sir.” He was ordered to
bring another pair.
“Bring your darbies!” cried Jane. “Yes,
bring ’em, but if you put them on me, sir, I’ll
drown you first chance I have, if I’ve only two
inches of water to do it in !”
Then, turning to the girls she clasped the
nearest of them to her, hysterically. “Oh, my
dears—don’t go with him —that man—that officer
- oh, girlies, let’s be true to one ’nother—- let’s
keep straight!’’
# # #
But—a soldier of the guard tore her away.
And the Sergeant himself placed the darbies on
the strong wrist that had worked such havoc.
But —the darbies being on, did Jane Applegarth
ever get an opportunity of carrying out her
threat, or, having got pije, did she avail herself of it?
She was granted the one—at least there were
nine inches of water olf Limeburners’ Point —and
she had the other—-that is to say, she drowned
Bankes, Officer and Gentleman, off the Point. The
manner thereof we will tell you some other day.
Before she drowned him, she had by his orders
been shaved, branded, flogged !
Of such were the Men of the Regime.
PRICE WARUNG.
Forbes slashes at Wales over the Gordon-
Cumming affair —asks how it is that the Prince,
if so convinced of Cumming’s guilt as to condemn
him, was content to allow him, a thief, to remain
in the Guards and in the clubs to which they
mutually belonged ?
Sir James Leo Steere, Speaker of the
Assembly, will fill the position of Acting-Governor
of Western Australia during the temporary
absence of Sir William Robinson, who has got
three months’ leave to visit London. lie repre-
sents the old Conservative element, and there is
much indignation in Perth at the prospect of an
Acting-Governor of such pronounced Conserva-
tive views.
Dear Bulletin, —I have just read Stan-
ley’s letter to the Times, dated from Washington,
B—l2—'oo. Perhaps, like Lord (in posse) Henry
Parkes, he does not read The Bulletin. But
many Bulletin readers see the Times, and I wish
to call their attention to the curious position in
which Henry Morton Stanley places himself. He
commences fairly by upholding British enterprise.
Was his a British affair? Then he states :
Beyond and above any personal question whatever
stands the honour of the English name.
What have poor Scotland and Ireland done to be
left in the cold ? And the joke is that H.M.S.
(by the way, those letters do for a lot of spittle-
licking) is, if hiled and the Yankee skin scrubbed
off, a Welshman. So the leek-eaters are out of it
also. They’ve no honour to hold above the
butcher’s “ personal question,” whatever that may
be. Now read this :
On the other hand, there have been many English
explorers, from my revered master, David Livingstone,
down to my own comrades in the advance-guard of this
expedition, who have united in quite a singular degree
gentleness with valour.
When was Livingstone Stanley’s master ? and in
what ? I don’t think he taught him bunkum and
bravado and butchering to begin, and snuffle to
end? And he, D.L., was a Scotchman. How
well I remember when he addressed the students
at home and each one had a slip of paper pre-
sented to him on which were some dog-Latin
verses by Jimmy Neaves (son of Lord Neaves).
Livingstone was Scotch enough then. The poem
began :
In hallo communi quum speaket Livingstonus,
Si quis studens clamabit aut utteret groanas
Aut picchabitur peasas aut aliquid throwando
Aut benches aut flooros aut uprowum kickando
Creahit annoyance ad alios studenses
Senati oh ! gemini! him bringabit ad senses.
And so on. Then Stanley goes on with lots more
about English actions, and near the end says :
Africa is inhabited not by timid Hindoos or puny Aus-
tralians. ■
There you are, you A.N.A.’s, and listen :
It is by moral superiority, first of all, that that control
must be won, as it has been won, and must be main-
tained, as far as any white man can hope to maintain it.
Yours truly,
Henry Morton Stanley.
Washington, Dec. 8, 1890.
Now, I was in Africa and there was a man called)
as far as I remember, H. M. Stanley there, and
lie wore store clothes and jack-boots, and spoke
(put on) through Ids nose, and was an honorary
member of a mess (Madras Sappers) in which, I
also had the privilege of eating my rations and
sand and drinking my allowance of a gallon and
a half of aqua distillata per diem. But I was
standing a bottle of Sirapkin (Indian for alleged
champagne) to one Lee, of the Royal Engineers,
and unluckily was called out on duty. The fizz
had been appropriated by what Lee called “That
damned cad of the New York Herald,” and
absorbed by “moral superiority.” About a week
afterwards one of my (Government) mules had his
control iron and maintained by some infernal
camp-follower of the Welsh - Yankee-English-
whitewash type. I forget his name, but his initials
were N.Y. Herald. It is such pleasing mi inis-
conces as these that make history and a proud
name for a hero.—Yours, Scotty the Wrinklkk.
P.S.- I often get them on a stiing now with “I
was with Stanley in Africa.” So I was— in ’fiS,
Abyssinia. Boree Creek, April 1,1891.

Dear Bulletin, — Rodney Claude Spencer,
who was condemned to death for murder, but
whose sentence has now been commuted to
“ life,” did not, as appears to be generally sup-
posed, belong to an aristocratic family. “ Rodney
Claude Spencer” is only a fancy name of his.
“ Jimmy Donaldson ” was nothing if not “flash.”
At one time his alias was very heavy and cumber-
some, and ran—“ Christopher Augustus Rodney
Claude Spencer,” but after a time he dropped the
Christopher Augustus, and kept the rest. In a
tree growing on the bank of a creek which runs
into the Roper River, N.T., there is an inscrip-
tion (overgrown now, I expect) setting forth the
virtues of the said C. A. R. C. Spencer, alias
Jimmy Donaldson ; also the fact that he was
known as the Aramae circus-rider. He was
clever on horseback, although not a good rider.
Now I come to think of it, poor Barney Keighran
lies buried close to the same tree. Jimmy is a
Queenslander, and has followed a variety of
occupations. He was something of a runner, and
once had a match with one George Cook, the
result whereof was a lot of ill-feeling between
R. C. .Spencer and several of the old hands.
One of these met him one day and “got onto
him ” about the race, and from words they came
to blows. Rodney could talk, but when it came
to fighting he generally trusted to his speed, and,
as a rule, got well out of danger. He ran well in
this instance, and his enemy followed him. There
was a good-sized water-hole near where the dis-
pute took place, the banks of which were nice and
smooth. Rodney made for this and took a gentle
canter round, eventually getting so close to his
opponent from behind that ho thought it time to
turn back and meet him at the other end. This
did not last very long—the “ pursuer ” was soon
exhausted, while the “ pursued ” was perfectly
fresh ; and then the absurdity of the situation
dawned upon the “old hand,” who sat down and
swore. “To think,” as he afterwards said, “ that
I should have been such a fool as to rim after
him, and all them other coves standing, laughing
at me.” The N.T. was a jolly place in those days
but the “grog was terrible.” —Elsie.

The Baccarat Scandal.
Amongst other French papers, LTngenue has
given its version of the baccarat scandal started
at Tranby Court We make the following free
translation :
“Odds fish!” (hereditary oath of the Royal
family of England) cried the Prince of Wales,
rolling his “ r’s ” as habitual with him when in a
passion or excited by a more tender sentiment.
“Odds fish, Sir Cummings, you have cheated!”
“ A thousand million sabretaches, my Lord,” re-
plied Sir Cummings, “you have lied.” “Let
him be conducted to the Tower,” ordered the heir
to the Crown, doubtless forgetting in his rage
that the Tower was no longer a prison but the
convenient and comfortable residence of an
American millionaire.
“The Duke of Coventry—to whom persons
seldom speak first—was consulted, and finally a
compromise was arrived at. ‘ Zounds, Sir Cum-
mings, ’ said the Prince, ‘ you must sign a paper
by which you bind yourself to commit suicide in
three months, and we swear to you that we will
faithfully keep the secret. On this condition our
friendship will be maintained according to the
code of honour practised in England.’ Sir Cum-
mings signed the fatal paper, put on his dress-suit
for dinner, and all of them continued to live in
the most amicable relations to the end.
“ But one will ask how the incident occurred ?
“ This is how.
“The financial position of the Prince leaves
much to be desired. Thus he finds himself com-
pelled to travel about to the ‘ country houses ’ of
rich parvenus, carrying with him his carpet-bag,
a green cloth and some counters. Asa rule the
stakes are modest, but on this occasion they were
very considerable. They played for ‘ bobs ’ and
demi-bobs ’ (as they say among the big people of
England). The Prince kept the bank, and, having
lost the ‘coup,’ paid a ‘bob’ to Sir Cummings,
who said to him, ‘ My Lord, you still owe me two
‘bobs.” It was then that the Prince exclaimed
* Oddsfish.’ It was thus that the storm burst.
“At the end of the three months agreed upon
Sir Cummings did not blow out his brains, and it
was then that the secret was divulged.
“What,* then, had prevented Sir Cummings
from faithfully carrying out his word ?
“He went to visit Monsieur Stead, the vir-
tuous proprietor of the World, a political journal
and a competitor of the Times, and this worthy
man assured him that cheating at a game was not
a crime involving suicide. ‘Co and consult Sam
Lewis, our great lawyer,’said Stead, ‘and Lord
Charles Russel, the celebrated barrister.’
“The interview with Mr. Sam Lewis was very
short. ‘ Sign me this small piece of paper, ’ Sam
said to him, presenting him with a sheet on which
were three letters—l. O.U.
“ Sir Cummings signed. Now the case will be
carried before the Queen’s Bench and tried before
f a jury of matrons.’
“What the Prince most fears is having to
appear before this jury of matrons.
“ The poor Sir Cummings has recently signed so
many little bits of paper that if one meets him he
hastens to say ‘Do you wish me to sign any-
think ?’
“ Look for the woman. This is the last word
about the incident. They assure us that Lady
Cummings, the young and beautiful wife of Sir
Cummings—but hush ! we decline to reproduce
the scandalous reports that are openly circulated
in London.”

tary Co. up to January, 1891, is £2,936,000.

Good old Alfred !
The prison newspaper is becoming an
American institution. It began in Elmira gaol,
New York, with a journal called the Summary,
edited by a literary burglar. The thing proved
so beneficial that since this time, 1883, most of
the big gaols have their newspaper, gratis, amid
the prisoners. The editor is allowed the run of
the best current newspaper literature, but it’s'the
judicious gaol-news that takes. Of course, the
Society racket can’t be carried too far.

has a foreman named Macduff.
Melbourne Herald moralises thus; —
“The reputation of a man is like his shadow-
gigantic when it precedes him, and pigmy in its
proportions when it follows.” Suppose he happens
to turn round ?
Justice Innes, in sentencing three young
men to five years each for assaulting a loose
woman at Sydney, expressed hisj great wonder
that*the tragic result of the Mount Rennie case
“had not been a solemn warning to the youth of
the colony.” The Mount Rennie catastrophe has
been a solemn warning—to juries, as in the case
before Judge Innes they ignored the capital
charge, which Was proven in at least two cases,
and found a verdict on the lesser count.
Ask for Wolfe’s Schnapps anfl take no other.
John Wilson, a middle-aged man, sen-
tenced to death at Ballarat for assaulting girl
six years old, had been convicted twice previously
and had served 10 years with two floggings, and
six months for similar offences. You can’t flog
the kinks out of a man’s brain.
The Yankee Government didn't seem to
care a cent (why should it ?) whether the Italian
Minister went home or stayed at Washington.,
Which reminds us that a liqe in “ Moths ” at
Melbourne Royal is serving as a .first-class topical
“gag.” “Your American wit is at fault; you
are not now in your own country,” says Prince
Zouroff to Fuchsia Mull, when that uneopventional
duchess threatens to take away his bruised
Yera. “No, in my country they lynch 'such
creatures as you,” chips in Fuchsia. And the
virtuous pit rises at her like a piece of puff-pastry
rises in the oven.
“THE BULLETIN.”
(Published at SYDNEY, N.S.W.)
The Unique Weekly—Australia’s Nonpareil
Illustrated Newspaper.

Is the only paper tvhieh has a consistent and dis-

, The Chinese; :
Imperial Federation.
The public eye rejects as 'uninteresting more
than half of what appears in the newspapers of
the day. It is only the remainder that is found
in The Bulletin.
The Bulletin is air aggressive Democratic
paper which strives ,to exclude from its- artisti-
cally-condensed columns all matter which is nob
of general human interest. The Bulletin was
started eleven years ago with no capital bub brains>
and has become a vast property, because it pos.
sesses a vitality lacked by the countless news-
paper-ventures in which Australian capitalists
have unavailingly spent large fortunes.
The Bulletin’s conductors claim that every
progressive paper on the Australian continent has
more or less endeavoured to profit by its example
and follow its lines. The result of its powerful
influence and its unprecedented literary and com-
mercial success can at once be seen by comparing
the methods pursued by the Australian daily and
weekly press prior to and immediately after the
first issue of The Bulletin.
From the date of The Bulletin’s inaugural
publication its success was assured. It had at
once a dozen more or less servile imitators, many
of which even took, so far as was possible, its
name. They are now ail dead, while The Bul-
letin continues the one phenomenal success in
Australian journalism, the ofie completely non-
local and distinctly universal Australasian news-
paper.
The Bulletin proprietary have spent some
thousands of pounds in fitting their buildings
with the latest appliances for high-class printing
and the production of illustrations, and they have
now issued The Bulletin in an entirely new
form—printed with new type of improved legi-
bility on the finest paper, and adorned with
sketches from the hands of the ablest and best-
paid newspaper artists Australia has ever seen.
The Bulletin is the only well-established Aus-
tralian journal which, throughout its earefer, has
been consistently Radical. It points with pride
to the "fact that' for years it stood alone among
secular newspapers in advocating the Irish
National Cause, and that it was the only Aus-
tralian journal which denounced the Soudan
Expedition from its inception.
The Bulletin is the beacon-fire of national
progress: it is the forceful summons to the
crusade against the monopolies in land, wealth,
power, and privilege—the direct cause of half the
misery which now afflicts British humanity.
Against the claims of avaricious clergy who olaiip
to monopolise salvation, against the dark despotism
of grasping plutocrats, and against the exercise of
any human authority which has*not been granted
by the people over whom it is exercised* The
''Bulletin will fearlessly and ruthlessly contend.
The Bulletin, unlike the ordinary weekly
journal, is not loaded with a weight of matter
transferred from th 6 columns of a daily paper
issued from the same office. The Bulletin is
not connected with any other paper, and the
matter it contains is all specially prepared by ifa
own unequalled staff.
The Bulletin, which is the only paper sold
by runners pa the streets of every Australasian
capital,de the most widely-circulated 6f all papers
published south of the equator, and is the only’
Australasian publication which enjoys, a popu-
larity Unhampered by the political boundaries
that so closely restrict other journals tO the
colonies in which they are'printed.
The Bulletin’s red cover is equally fapjiliar
to the bushman of the Far North, the stockman'
of Central Australia, the pearl-shejler. of Torres
Straits, and the digger m the New Zealand
Ranges. A paper which is at once the roost
popular oity publication and the organ of the
intelligent bushman"must indeed be broadly based
Perhaps the strongest proof of The Bulletin's
popularity is found in the unchallengeable fact—
easily ascertainable from any postal official —that,
of all journals issued in Australasia, The Bul-
letin is the one most frequently remailed from
one reader to another.
The Bulletin is the journal which, of all Aus-
tralian journals, is mosji frequently quoted in Eng-
land, America, and the colonies.
The Bulletin is going ahead faster than any
other paper, because its* vast mass of original and
specially-written reading matter is the brightest
and best, its illustrations the cleverest and most
artistic, and its policy the boldest and most
straightforward ever submitted to the Australian
public.

Noble-street, Newton, Geelong,
10th December, 1888.
To H. F. Kugelmann, Esq.
Dear Sir,—This is to certify that I, Patrick Keenan, was
taken ill about three years ago with a severe cold, which
turned into Bronchitis and became chronic. 1 consulted
more than one doctor, and during that time took a great
quantity of medicine without it doing me the slightest
good, but I became worse every day, wasting away until
1 became a perfect skeleton. Ahou t February last I began
to spit up blood, and what with the cough and severe
pains in my left lung, and the quantity of bad matter I
was expectorating every day, I became very much alarmed.
Retiring of your professional skill I consulted you, and
you told me you could cure me for a given sum. I agreed
ami went under your treatment in March last. At the
end of three months I was taken bad one morning, and
vomited about two pints of blood, matter and stringy
phlegm. Prom that time I commenced to improve, anil
was gaining in weight every day. About a month ago the
blood ceased coming, the cough disappeared, and the
lung healed completely, and thank God and your pro-
fessional skill, I am as well as ever.
P. KEENAN.
Declared and subscribed before me, one of Her Majesty’s
Justices of the Peace, for the Southern Bailiwick, in the
County of Grant, this 10th day of December, 1888.
Peter Smytiie, J.P.
